So I guess it's a case of 'Duh, no shit Sherlock, hope you had fun doing all that thrashing around proving a known point'.

The current little sleepy backwater Alpha test-bed Stanton system with it's fractious little factions has no idea about the storm that is going to roll through the UEE systems. Go read the history wall of Hurston Dynamics, a major arms manufacturer for the UEE military, there is no mention of the Vanduul war on that wall - yet.

About a year ago now Jared Huckaby told me in person that the Terra Nova system was going to be the first Alpha test system for the PU, but they changed their minds. That's what all the initial work on Terra Prime landing zone and SataBall was all about. That's why there is a huge full wall mural of Terra Prime in the meeting room of the LA office that we've seen on CIG discussion videos, that's why there was a very early video of the first person view of a player running around Terra Prime. They haven't dumped anything, the Terra Nova system will be in the game and all that comes with it, they just changed the communities focus to the Stanton system because what's the point in giving away spoilers for a system they aren't bringing to the players yet?

We also know that they must have expanded the Squadron 42 story line from the 2012 Kickstarter as well because a 2013 stretch goal for 10 'extra star systems' included the Odin system as one of the 10. So why is the Odin system featured in the Squadron 42 vertical slice? Because the story was changed to a time before that Vega II attack to allow us to form an attachment to our character before the main event starts.

You might also notice that every time they show something from Squadron 42 they always show a date and time as well as the location of the events. There are no time jumps in Squadron 42, if you want to get to a star system you have to go through the intervening star system jumps. The route from Stanton to the Odin system goes through Pyro and Nyx. The only landing zone completed to a point where players could use it when they switched from using Terra Nova to the Stanton system was Delamar / Levski and that's why Delamar was put into the wrong star system. You can get to Odin via the same route from Terra through Magus to Stanton.

Star Citizen is not going to be some disconnected multiplayer sandbox tacked on to a single-player game, it is the continuation of the story, and your role in that story if you partook of Squadron 42, you will have an emotional connection to it - to the history, places and characters in it. That's why the level of detail and immersion has to be full on, because the plan is to keep hitting you in 'the feels' again and again throughout the story arc of this game universe. If the attack on Vega II is the equivalent of the Germans going into Poland in 1939 or France in 1940, that directly lead to that speech in the Houses of Parliament, then what's coming next? Admiral Bishop starts his speech by referring to the period of appeasement by the UEE doesn't he? It was Germany going into Poland that prompted the British Empire aligned nations to declare war, which is what Admiral Bishop is doing, so where is that battle of France equivalent going to take place that brings the enemy right up to the gates?

Simple and easy can be done quickly. Those that stick to the tired routine ways of faking it know that well. Bethesda have far more resources than CIG, and their project has been in development for a similar length of time with a barely updated Creation Engine, yet they have opted to stick to the usual cop out mechanics. No manual flying from space to ground, no walking around inside your ship while in flight. No EVA, no boarding, no ship to ship docking, no refuelling (they explained they "have made ships with unlimited fuel because they didn't want players to get frustrated", sounds a lot like one of those "you'd get bored" excuses doesn't it?). Starfield touts that they have 100 star systems - but they only have 4 built cities to visit in those 100 star systems. The cell technology to give the impression that you can land anywhere on a planetary body is just an extension of the cell technology in Skyrim V updated because the hardware is better. Of course what few know is that Bethesda could draw on years of modders doing free work on their engine to demonstrate how to extend it's capability.

You keep comparing this project to other games that don't even come close to the same level of complexity and capability this project has now, and you don't even know what the final version (or Squadron what 42 has) is going to be.

Chris Roberts decided (with the full backing of the community) that we would go for as little faking it as is possible. That's why there was no-one saying "You'd get bored of walking around in, and out of your ships interior after the 10th time."

It's the most common statement made by new players when they come to the project.

Sadly as usual the points included do not even reference the large NPC population that will inhabit the Star Citizen game universe. There is also no mention of how detailed and all-encompassing the player reputation and history logs will be. You also neglected to inform the Chat GPT AI that there is a societal structure in the Star Citizen universe, where you go and what you can do will be dependent on your social status and security status - Citizenship, even if you have earned it or bought it, can be revoked by the UEE for lawless behaviour. There are prison sentences, and eventually there will be a prison planet, so expect longer punishments compared to now. Unlike now a criminal record, even though you have served your time, will persist.

Part of the reason the current game universe seems like a free-for-all wild west is because many of the systems are not yet in, and because of CIG's reluctance to be overly punitive, also there is no permanency to a players actions - if a player knows their record will be permanent, they are likely to be more cautious in their actions. They won't be able to access insurance / replacements as easily as lawful players. Player who have Squadron 42 and retire that Squadron 42 character to the verse will have more privileges like access to mil-spec equipment and material, and because of their higher security rating also certain locations and mission types will be restricted to only them. When you accept security missions from the Advocacy in the game now there is no restrictions even though the mission description refers to 'those with the relevant experience', this will be different in the future.

The game is about finite resources, managing those resources to progress toward the goal an individual has set for themselves. The NPC's will be a drain on the lawless player, whether they think they can identify between a player or NPC's or not is irrelevant. Secure systems will have security NPC's and they have infinite resources, which will respond to the level of crime reported in an area. Large herds of wildebeest still roam the African Savannah, and large herds of Carribou still roam northern Canada, Russia and Europe even though there are predators that hunt both those species - the percentage chance that any individual herding beast being attacked is very low. At range a player will not be able to distinguish between players and NPC ships and those same NPC's will use POI to POI route finding which a player can choose not to - the game space is huge, the chances of encounter outside of those trade routes are very very small. Which action seeking pirate is going to wait around for hours in the middle of nowhere on the off-chance of seeing a player?

There will be an up-to-the-minute security status warning system in place so that lawful players wishing to engage in their chosen activity can choose to go to a location or not based upon that information

The game will also have static security checkpoints to get around like the ones players just walk through because they are not functioning, there will be more, unlawful players will have to spend time and resources to get around those checkpoints - any time and resources spent dealing with NPC's and methods to circumvent security checkpoints is time and resources away from interfering with players.

Those players who simply like the PvP action can also play in support of security forces as bounty hunters, and while they can move about freely and can call on NPC resources the lawless player will not have those same benefits. They can chase lawless players around and the hunters become the hunted.

The game universe will have organised events - like Murray Cup Racing, IAE and ILW to name but a few. Lawless players will also be restricted in where they go - the Helios system for example has a paradise holiday planet, but Helios is also heavily secured as a UEE military outpost.

It is about striking a balance, and there will be 'game masters' monitoring everything going on in the game universe - if the balance is too far one way or the other they have many tools to redress that imbalance, they can close Jump Gates, increase security, restrict the flow of resources. They can even cause local natural disasters.

The economy will react to events in the game, if lack of security makes goods and services too expensive that will be dealt with. The UEEN will protect strategic resources as it feels it is required.There should never be a zero-risk scenario for any player whatever activity they choose to engage in, using your intelligence and wit to survive in such an environment is all part of the fun of life in the Star Citizen universe.

When all the systems are finally in place many who think they will have a high old time being the poacher will realise it is easier, more profitable and less restricting to become the gamekeeper - for every defection from the lawless camp that means double the security for the lawful player. Only the very dedicated will continue a lawless life. I very very rarely get attacked in the game now, and security right now is the worst it is ever going to be.

This is an interesting bug / problem because it gives us clues about the process by which things are brought into the game universe.

Prior to any form of global persistence we had player persistence first - what you had equipped the last time you logged out should be on you when you log back in, logging out caused the DGS to record your last state to a database, so you are stowed. Then we had shipboard persistence, anything stowed aboard ship came back when you logged out/in again and brought your ship back to the pad. But if your ship was unstowed then you had to claim it via insurance and so lost it's contents because the server had no function to record it's state outside of a hangar, until bed logging.

A player is a collection of items to load into the game - first come 'the bones' that allow you to move and do stuff - they are invisible. Next, attached to those bones are a 3D model (mesh) of your body - one for your body and one for your head. That body and head are parented to your bones (the bones are the parent and the body meshes are attached to the bones). Next any gear you have is attached first, flight suit or clothes, the helmet chest armour and arm and leg armour are attached to the flight suit, the armour pieces have attachment points for other equipment. Your head has the HUD element attached to it. The helmet also has a HUD element attached to it.

The way it used to work is that a server was spun up with a pristine level designer made version of the Stanton system and every Stanton system on every server was identical. Once that was loaded onto a server other services would then load in other elements like NPC's from the NPC scheduler service and players (from some service I can't remember the name of so I'll just call it the player item attribution service). Player items include you (your avatar) and anything attributed to you on your RSI Hangar page. Anything not attached to you goes in the local parent inventory.

The Dedicated Game Server running the game would update your record of where you are and what you have in the game to a database if you stowed those player items before log out - which means making a hard copy record of where they were and what they were attached to. When the player loads into a landing zone you are attached to that entity which is the landing zone which is attached to the planet which is attached to the game universe entity. This is what is called Object Containers. As long as there is a record of you being present in an object container on a database the DGS can access then that data can be retrieved the next time you log in, if there is no record, then there is nothing to attach you to and you are 'unstowed'.

We are seeing lot's of evidence of network errors that are either not making entries to databases (like PES) or perhaps corrupted entries to databases. But the network issues also cause slowdown of retrieval of information that IS present on databases. We see this most commonly as control input to reaction time lag, inventory lag etc. So it's all part of the same problem.

They've switched from static databases that are updated from running DGS's to the constantly running persistence database which is currently being fed from multiple instances of the Stanton system at once - hundreds of them, perhaps even more. There is a temporary 'patch fix' service running on the DGS feeding data to and from the PES service. That patch fix seems to because issues with the network stack queue between the DGS, PES and the player clients, and other services. The change to the way we get our ships now is a fix to reduce the network traffic caused by players claiming ships at multiple locations, many at a time for those who have fleets of them.

The fix where you load into a non-game-universe standalone module to refresh the database holding your player items suggests if it works then it wasn't just network slowdown that threw up the 'player unstowed' problem... but the long load time to get into the game once you tried the fixes says there is network slowdown of retrieval of player attributed data as well, which would fit because we're seeing evidence of DGS to backend service network slowdown all over the place, and the only answer to network slowdown is the same as it used to be on dial up networking in the 1990's - all you can do is wait and hope.

The fully persistent universe they have planned includes the persistence database, but also the Replication Layer Gateway Service through which all input services feed information to running server shards and PES simultaneously. The persistence database is in but the Replication Layer isn't yet. When it does go in then each DGS will report to that persistently running service so that each server isn't making entries to the PES database individually. which should / will improve matters significantly.

sigh - please show me another game that has something similar to what Cloud imperium Games are calling The Replication Layer Gateway Service - Replication Layer for short.

If you read the information from CIG they put out about how they plan to implement full persistence and server meshing you'll see a graphic, it is a map of how the services connect to each other. You'll see that game clients will connect to the Replication Layer, the Replication Layer then connects to the DGS shards and the persistence database - there is no direct connection between player clients and DGS shards. The Replication Layer Gateway Service has a function to synchronise the 'state' of the DGS's and the Persistence database and then replicate that state to each individual connected game client.

The Replication Layer Gateway Service is not yet implemented - but PES is - parts of it's function are still running on each individual DGS shard which means multiple DGS's are trying to synchronise with the persistence database - and they are getting out of sync. The persistence database started running when 3.18.0 went live and continued running until they reset it with 3.18.1. No DGS shards run continuously - none of them. That global synchronisation function will be broken off the DGS build and taken over by the Replication Layer Gateway Service which will also run continuously.

Read the output from the dev's regarding the issues with 3.18.0 - they don't talk about server performance or tick rates, they do talk about databases, services and network, and they actually use the term 'out of sync'.

The problem is more that there is a service missing, a function that is currently running on the individual DGS's - The Replication Layer Gateway Service. Once that goes in it's job is to keep everything in sync rather than multiple DGS's trying to keep everything in sync. That function will be taken off the DGS build.

Relating Star Citizen to the way other multiplayer games work is of no use, Star Citizen doesn't work like other multiplayer games. I'm well aware that the way networked multiplayer games work is written in stone, but CIG threw those stones away and re-wrote the book.

The answer is yes and no. They can do things which improve the backend service network performance - and they have - but yes Server Meshing will alleviate that network strain even more because servers that only hold a small part of the game world don't need to update all the change states of entities in that game world, only the one's that are in that local area of the game world.

The reason this problem showed up was because currently all shards hold multiple versions of the Stanton system, when server meshing goes in there won't be so man multiple versions of the game world running simultaneously.

It does, but those effects are in the few milliseconds, not many seconds or even minutes.

An example:
Players were reporting that they requested ATC and the hangar doors appeared to open, so the player flies in because the DGS told them the ATC command was granted and the doors are open - but when the DGS queries the persistence database it says the hangar doors were not open the ship hit the doors and was destroyed - so the player may even have reached the landing pad before the PES updated DGS says - your ship hit the doors and exploded. That's how badly out of sync the DGS's were getting with the backend services.

If that were due to DGS tick rate then that server tick rate would have to have been at multiple thousands of milliseconds per tick.

I have been telling people for a while now what causes interaction delay and 'change state' delay, and no it doesn't have anything (much) to do with server tick rate. It's because of delay in network traffic between the player client and server, server and backend services (like the PES set of services), then network back out to connected player clients.

There are only a small number of movement controls that are directly controlled by the player (input commands), most player inputs which are 'change state requests' go up to the server first, get collected, sorted and sent to the backend services then the results of those 'change states' are sent out to each connected client by the server simultaneously so hopefully all players see those change state events at the same time. Change state events are terminals, doors open/close, hangar doors, ATC requests, weapon customisation, player gear changes equip/unequip, elevators and loot boxes etc.

People say 'this thing is not working!' like calling ATC, or getting out of your chair, or getting out of bed, , turning ships lights on, or even the delay in an NPC realising the player is there or missions not completing - they may or may not be bugged but those of us who wait before spamming more commands (because we know why it'a happening) will find that the command does work there is just a variable wait delay before you see the result of your input - this is caused by network delays, not server tick rates. These network delays have been mentioned in dev posts and patch notes.

If you've had a 30K you'll know the game becomes unresponsive to most commands because those commands made by player input don't happen client side first, if they did happen client side first we'd all see the results of player actions at different times and as they said the persistence database 'got out of sync' with player activity and that is why it needs resetting, with a 30K they need the network connection to the server which is missing when there is a server crash. They have made a DGS Crash recovery service which uses the persistence database to spin up servers to replace the one that crashed in it's virtually identical state, when they talked about this they said the player may experience periods of game unresponsiveness for a short while until the recovered server shard is spun up and connected to all backend services.

The reason they changed the way ship ordering is done at ASOP terminals when you first log into the game is because players were spawning in for the first time and flying to a space station then requesting all their ships to be transferred to the space station all at once by going down the list (I was guilty of this and I have over 30 ships) - this caused the 'entitlement processing' backend servers to get overloaded with network traffic - which lead to ASOP terminal issues and inventory issues, even player log in flow issues.

Anyone who has been following Elon Musk could work out, every project he has been involved with is about that colony on Mars. Can't have ICE powered cars on Mars. The hyperloop is fairly pointless and impractical on Earth, but it would be perfect for Mars. Need better, cheaper solar panels and batteries on Mars. Need a tunnel boring machine on Mars for habitats and underground highways as well as mineral mining, even the robot is about Mars. Starlink is a double-plus, they can use it on Mars too but it also has the advantage on Earth that no government or pesky human can interfere with the information flow - cables are vulnerable, constellations of satellites aren't as easily taken out, and we know how he feels about governments and their oversight. He isn't the biggest fan of 'democracy in action'. Notice he isn't interested in anything that flies in atmosphere except rocket powered engines.

Once they reach the asteroid belt there's gold aplenty in them thar hills. Way more than there is down on this ball of rock and it isn't locked up with private land owners or governments. Once they get there it's easier to extract, it's getting there and staying long enough to start operations that is the issue. Once they do though they don't need anything from Earth anymore except warm bodies for a while. Plenty of rare Earth minerals out there and no restrictions on nuclear power with unlimited cloudless sunlight.

He mooted the idea of taking fissile radioactive material to Mars, all the internet nerds laughed at the idea because they assumed he thought about spreading it over the surface. But it's more likely he'd tunnel under the polar ice cap and dump it down the very deep hole. It would heat the surrounding rock if there was enough of it, the outcome would be to melt the polar ice - which is mainly CO2 and water - thickening the atmosphere, but if the sub-surface rock did melt the heavy radio-active material would sink down further into the core - again big 'if' there was enough to make the core rotate again then Mars has a magnetosphere perhaps enough to stop atmospheric loss into space (a thicker atmosphere would reduce cosmic radiation too) and reduce solar radiation at the surface. There would have to be a lot of it, but there's plenty out there in the asteroid belt and there may even be some on Mars' surface anyway. It would be many many years, perhaps even hundreds before that plan could work...

Here's something to ponder on.

Those people who now come into the forums accusing some of us of being White Knights for Chris Roberts were back in 2014 somewhat haughtily telling people in the community that Chris was right, there won't be flight to ground and free roam around planets and the rest of the community simply didn't understand how games were made. They were absolutely right of course - until the thing with CryTek happened. Some of them couldn't adjust, they felt betrayed. That's where the 'scope creep' meme came from because they knew what a massive undertaking it would be to get it to work in what they 'knew' the game was going to be.

Ironic eh?

People use this term 'scope creep' as though it is some in-arguable fact. It isn't, it was a meme dreamt up by those who were disgruntled that they were in the minority when a single key change happened, unfortunately they were some of the main players in the community at the time. If you go back to the days of 10 for the Chairman you'll hear their game handles being mentioned a lot. They were used to expecting to speak to the development team directly, they even used to email them very regularly. Like daily in some cases. Through their barrage of CS tickets they deliberately steered the marketing team to allow exploits of the RSI store functions - the buy to melt mechanic for example, which have had to be patch fixed to regular players inconvenience ever since. These guys weren't stupid. They were smart cookies who could think ahead. In those days the marketing division were saying yes to just about anything they asked for. So there's the irony, the scope creep in marketing and it's complexity was directly caused by those very same people now accusing CIG of scope creep.

So what happened?

Up to late 2014 Cloud Imperium Games - Chris Roberts in particular had said that there wouldn't be any free flight down to planets (because he knew CryEngine didn't have that capability at that time). There was going to be an auto-land (in reality a loading screen just like in the Flight of The Nova and what is coming to Starfield) function to go down to spaceports on planets and each landing zone was going to be a hand made fixed size play space. Like other games made with CryEngine, the 'invisible walls' or encircling high un-clime-able mountains to stop you going off the edge of the map. Erin and Chris were talking about security domes (like in Arena Commander) to stop players trying to fly out of landing zones. The community generally didn't like this, they didn't like it at all.


CryTek were having cashflow issues and the dev team had not been paid for months. Sean Tracy originally worked as 'Roving Ambassador' for the CryTek R&D team so he was seconded to Cloud Imperium Games but he resigned from CryTek and came to work for CIG. Members of the CryTek R&D team all resigned and Brian Chambers decided to set up their own development studio in Frankfurt, he contacted Chris and asked about the possibility of them being offered work on Star Citizen - obviously we know what happened there. Chris was very keen to get such experienced CryEngine people, it was a golden opportunity not to be missed, anyone who would have found out about it later if it hadn't happened would now be berating Chris for not taking that opportunity when he had the chance.

One of the R&D dev team 'Hannes' had been following the discussion about players wanting to be able to fly down to planets and free roam about those planets and moons (in early 2016 I was at BritizenCon where Brian talked to us about what happened), the community hadn't been happy with a video CIG had shown that showed the Auto-land down to Area 18 so in his own time he set about seeing if he could do what the community was begging for, he made it then he presented it to Chris.

So, there they were, Chris had his Squadron 42 project in the bag now as far as he was concerned the multiplayer was the 'fans and backers' game, the original plan was for the multiplayer to be a 'drop in drop out' function of Squadron 42. When he made his Kickstarter pitch he could not have known what was going to happen at CryTek, indeed the speed and amount of funding the community was giving him took him completely by surprise. He knew he now had the opportunity to give the community what it was virtually demanding, imagine if he hadn't? The backlash from the community would be huge. Also the PU as it existed at first launch would not have happened, it would have taken longer to get a few landing zones hand made so that the players could get into the verse, he originally only planned the modules for players to sample before the game release. Were his original plans 'optimistic'? Very probably, which game developer has not overshot their planed release date?

Everything else that keeps getting referred to as 'scope creep' by the noisy few and then picked up by everyone else only came about for two reasons, to solve the extra functionality that full planets would need, such as Object Container Streaming, and to update the game engine to the more modern standards, for example PBR - Physically Based Rendering - came to CryEngine around the end of 2014. Again if Chris has said 'no' to updating to PBR people would have howled in derision. Things like 64 bit precision, server meshing, a massive NPC population and full persistence were already part of the original plan, generally people just didn't know it. Watch the original launch trailer for Squadron 42, notice anything? Ships inside ships, which means physics grids inside a physics grid - you're not seeing a cut-scene of you being shot out of a launch tube to materialise in your cockpit, because that ships in ships feature was the original plan.

The 2016 version of Star Citizen as planned would have looked like an outdated version of Starfield... and those who think he should have stuck to his plan for that type of game and then built on it with expansions simply don't understand how those core tech features were fundamental to the final game once that decision was to put in full planets. They could not have gone back and re-built the game to get all todays features in, let alone all the ones that are coming.

Those who demand work on Squadron 42 be shelved (and a lot of them are those FUD-Spreaders) don't know what they are talking about, it was the community's version of the BDSSE and the requirement to have a playable Live iterative game release that has slowed everything down. Normally in game development the pretty graphics is the last thing they ad, all the money spent on Style Guides, lore, texturing, missions and even the expanded ship fleet and their functionality beyond Squadron 42 requirements was so that the community could have something to play while waiting. Every time there has been an opportunity to pull in more people to build the PU Chris has taken it with both hands, the Montreal office for example staffed by ex-Bethesda, ex-Ubisoft and even TV and movie industry developers are all working hard to put in features for the PU not required for Squadron 42.

Chris is very conscious that he was given the opportunity to build 'his' dream game far better than he was ever expecting by the community and so he felt an obligation to listen to the community's dreams. If we could supply the where-with-all (as we have) then he would commit to the many more years of his life to make it happen for 'our' game, not just 'his'. Those who like to compare to other games forget, how would we all respond if Chris was telling us "You'd get bored walking around inside your ship after the 10th time", how did ED fans respond to it? They all knew it was a BS cop-out. Unlike some in this community Chris sees this whole project as a partnership between the development team and the community, as clearly expressed by Brian Chambers. The Pupil To Planet video was their answer to the community regarding the fact that they heard us and what our version of the BDSSE should be.

I doubt there is a single member of the development who aren't also community members, every time we speak to them in person they are always talking about how they are making the community's game - not Chris Roberts game.

It will take years and years to do what the community was demanding (because they aren't knowledgeable about what it takes to do what they are asking) and so the Alpha iterative approach was the only one that could have worked. Taking the Alpha approach 'brick by brick' to give the community something to play while waiting is what has complicated and lengthened the development time significantly. since Chris knew this was going to be the case there was no reason not to have other features worked on because they don't delay the game at all, they're just things that add to the experience which they could do anyway because now they had more time.

Anyone who says things happened differently are lying FUD-spreaders with an axe to grind because they weren't the darlings of the dev team and top dogs in the community anymore, or they are the victims of those 'influencers' who think they 'made the project' in the early days simply regurgitating the crap they've been spreading.

It is mentioned as a bug in the patch notes: Actor Feature / Network category - player inputs may take many seconds to activate.

The way Star Citizen works is that you command your character to get up by holding Y, but the command is not executed locally on your PC game client that command goes to the server then back down to your character (there are good reasons for this). If there is network lag - which 3.18 has been plagued with - then your character appears to do nothing so people assume it isn't working and try pressing buttons again. Of course this all adds to the queue for network bandwidth.

When the game loads to your spawn bed, wait a while to let everything load in. Hold Y once - don't do anything else and just wait. Eventually your character will respond to the command. It's the same with turning engines off in your ship, opening doors etc. Almost all player commands work this way.

The long load times., the voids at stations and missing elevators is a PC game client load issue. Star Citizen requires a lot of RAM, more than 20GB at times. Even at minimum Windows takes more than 6GB RAM. If you have many memory resident background process running it is substantially more than 6GB. If you only have 16GB RAM this leaves the game less than 10GB free RAM. If you have a GPU with less than 8GB VRAM then any deficit of VRAM is transferred to RAM (which is slower to load than VRAM).

The first time the game runs on your system it takes time to compile shaders to the Shader Cache, this should be lessened on subsequent running of the game. If you interrupt the shader compile it will just start it all over again next time you run the game.

The partial fix for this lack of RAM is:
1. Make sure as few memory resident programs are running in the background as possible. Use the Task Manager.

2. Increase your Windows System Pagefile up to 32GB and place that pagefile on your fastest access storage drive. you should do this even if you have 32GB RAM installed, it will make your whole system run generally smoother.

Use the fastest access drive for the game files, it must be at least an SSD, preferably NVMe.

Also if you have NVidia Control Panel you can increase the Shader Cache Size in Settings - Manage 3D Settings - Global Settings to 10GB. If you are short of space on that drive and it is your OS drive then you can free up more space by reducing the Recycle Bin max size from it's default 10% for al drives to something like 10GB. You can also Google 'How to delete Hiberfil.sys' follow the simple instructions, restart your PC and that will free up as much disk space as you have installed RAM.

3. In the game go away from the area where there is missing geometry if you can, and return again after a minute or two, which will give that local geometry time to load into your system RAM. Unfortunately trains are hard on RAM loading because they go so fast lot's of geometry is being streamed into and out RAM very quickly. The name for this in-game system of loading game geometry into RAM is called Object Container Streaming - OCS. The Dedicated Game Servers that the game runs on also has a form of this (Server) Object Container Streaming - SOCS. There are two other types of managers in the game that control what is loaded into RAM, one is called Bind Culling and one is called 'Off Screen Culling' by walking away from an area, so that you can no longer see it, with missing geometry you are using 'Off Screen Culling' to unload that area from memory and re-loading it when you return.

I also think that landing zone markers should be at the spaceport, not the actual landing zone structures.

Hopefully the new Star Mao will have all those features.

Lot's of people are playing the game. I have played it and I'm not a streamer.

I'll give an example of the way I approach trying to figure out how CIG organises their systems.

We know they switched from the sequential read iCache to a graphed based database to suit the Object Container system better because they told us.

When people were first reporting 19K's, 30K's etc trying to log into the game some said they noticed that accounts with many items were getting those connection issues more often. My first reaction was to poo poo the idea, and I did reply to a post about it in that vein because my supposition of how the DGS's were accessing player account held items (including the player body) was that it had direct access to that attributed account item database per player on request.

CIG have never talked about how the account bound items are brought into the game as far as I'm aware so I'm in the dark.

When I thought about the player invisibility problem and the idea crept in that maybe the player body is loaded into the persistent item cache with the parent being the landing zone, which has parent planet, which has parent game universe, it made me re-evaluate the claim of those who said the log in issues were noticeably more frequent for players with lots of items because they would be most susceptible to time-outs.

If the process was that each item was requested by the DGS from the player attribution account cache 'on demand' by the player that log in delay idea simply wouldn't make sense.

Then a thought occurred to me.

You can only access account bound items in the local inventory of where you choose to spawn first and nowhere else, it could be CIG's choice or it could be a limitation of the system. If it's not CIG's choice this shouldn't be a limitation if the 'on demand' theory was correct. If the DGS was requesting from the player account database on demand you should be able to access that stuff anywhere in the game, but you can't.

Next the empty ASOP terminal bug shows up, no-one can access their attributed ships. Those ships are account bound, they come from the player attribution database, not the global item persistence database. Hmmm. OK.

So they are putting in the PES system right now and all these issues are showing up.

Another thought occurred to me:
What if on load into the game the player body is attached to the landing zone and all the players account bound items are also all loaded into the persistence database with the parent being the player's inventory attached to the landing zone? What an odd notion that the players face and body textures and hair might be items to attach to a parent body (basically only a game camera item)?

Now it makes sense - to me at least.
You can only access your gear from within the parent item - your spawn location's local inventory which is attached to the player body (which is really the players inventory when the player is attached to that location).

What if the code written to the persistence database for an item allowed an 'attach' function to a parent and a sub item attach function so it could be a parent itself? If the attach item code was fine and dandy, and the sub item attach code was corrupted you could get into the game but you wouldn't be able to attach anything to you. If the attach to parent code was busted you wouldn't be able to successfully log into the game because there'd be no confirmation of your arrival. To the system you are just another item it couldn't find so any network timeout on the loading in of your body would result in a failure to get into the game.

If corrupted data was being written into the persistence cache about your body maybe it would affect whether your body could be classed as a parent item? If it were so what would you see? Nothing attached to the player, not even the default gear.. or maybe even hair and textures?

If the persistence cache was not getting the ship items loaded into the local inventory of the player parent from the player attribution account database then there would be no ships on the local ASOP terminal because those ships were supposed to attach to the parent item 'player body'.

Ergo with this reasoning player invisibility and no ships are basically the same problem.

So my spit-balling supposition is, the DGS does not access player account attributed items 'on demand' they are all loaded into the persistence database at log in ready to be available to the player and the landing zone parent if the player discards them from their body or inventory. That's a lot of data if lots of players are logging in at once. This makes sense to me given the bugs that are showing up with the first implementation of PES. The fact that they have gradually got worse over time (or as player load has increased) suggests the constantly running pCache has gotten corrupted with a combination of bad writes and network issues. Hence the bug hunting and resets.

I don't have the skillset to know why network issues might cause bad writes to a static database, so my reasoning on the matter stalls there.

Yes, you're right the Replication Layer Gateway is made from several services as you say, I just lumped them in together as CIG do for brevity's sake. (I was making a little in-joke when I said the RL was a traffic cop, not a scribe.

I do write these posts off the top of my head, so I admit I might not be as clear in the details as it's possible to be. I also suffer from word blindness so sometimes when writing posts I forget the name of something and have to go find it or sit and wait for it to pop into my head after a while, I'm terrible with names, but understanding systems I am good at, but the overall idea comes across.

I mentioned in other posts above how CIG only talk about components if they are new and relevant, once they have a whole service they just mention the service, of course it depends who is doing the talking. What is now accepted as PES should actually have the acronym PESS because it's the Persistent Entity Streaming Service made of several components.

My aim in writing posts isn't absolute accuracy for those who already know as much, if not more than I, it's to try and describe the overall systems to those who find them hard to follow and don't understand why things are happening in the game. Some may think it but I'm not trying to points score, only further the discussion - if I am trying to be funny, or scathing, the style is completely different. Posts like yours for example if it wasn't directed at me i'd just read it and appreciate the information. I'm conscious there are other read only' people and so if I think a point needs clarifying I jump and do my best. It's a discussion and though i write my posts as if I'm stating fact that's only for brevity, if I'm wrong I'm happy for someone to come along and re-clarify - it is a discussion in a character limited forum post style that doesn't lend it's self to complex discussion, or presentation.

I don't know anything about CIG's account log-in flow for example, so i don't talk about that. I have no idea why people are getting 40K's.

A lot of it is gleaned from reading what the dev's say, and first read is always a brain ache - but then you start asking 'Why' what are they trying to achieve, what problem are they trying to solve and how did they say they solved a problem (especially when you see things in the game) then go read it (watch it) again, and again and the more you ask why is something happening in the game the more the read of the information makes sense. For example if players on a server are reporting NPC ships teleporting around why is it that when an elevator door opens (even though I'm on a US server rather than my home UK server) do players see elevator doors open at the same time as me? It strongly suggests there has to be some prioritisation going on, it can't just be server tick rate because that would affect everything. The elevator might be late, but the other players see it open when I do. If, as they have said, network overload is a problem at scale, then why not have the issue of players seeing certain things happen at different times, when there's obvious issue of lack of simultaneity with other things?

I have no firm idea why some players or ships are invisible, but if the parts that make up the players appearance, wearable items and face, are indeed stored on a cache and it's not being accessed properly, that would make sense. considering items are attached to item ports and those items are supposed to be persistent in the game, it leads to the conclusion it's the same item cache access problems as all the other issues. They're basically just the game camera now without items attached to them. They can't use anything because in order to use it it has to be flagged as unstowed and attached to a parent item, that's a problem if the DGS is querying the database and it's being told that parent item doesn't exist. the players are reporting that when invisible they can open their inventory and see items, because that parent item - the inventory - does exist on the database but they can't equip anything from that inventory. In my estimation this strongly suggests the player body is loaded as an in-game item on the persistence database normally but it has failed. The player attribution database is making corrupt entries to the pCahe about the players body, but not about other items they own.

Again I have no firm idea what they are talking about when they say 'Optimisations' but if that means re-prioritising some things in the network queueing stack because in the past it wasn't a big issue when they had less network bandwidth concerns, that again would make sense. In the PTU these issues didn't arise, there's no difference between server player populations and tick rate between Live and PTU from the DGS running and player point of view, but there's a very big difference between the network requirement of many many DGS's running needing bandwidth into that thing that determines the state of everything in the game.

I can't remember when exactly they changed the way commands were executed - years ago - but there was a lot of complaint about lag in the game at the time prior to it's implementation. Clive Johnson was the one who did a write up about the changes they made. There's a very easy way to check the voracity if my statement, log in, run around a bit, pull your network cable to simulate a 30K and you'll see what you can't do. If the results of your commands were only client side first there's no logical reason you can't do those things. I have checked some of the things you can't do while waiting for the 30K timeout notification. Example when there's a server client disconnect you can equip to your hand a medpen, but you can't use it. For a time players did report on the IC they could do they same, they could equip a medpen but they couldn't use it. It's the same with the utility tool, you can equip it but you can't use it during a server client disconnect. If you're noticing there is significant delay operating some game items like doors, and yet you get almost instant response to using a medpen when you know it only works while the client is connected to the server the logical conclusion is that there must be some prioritisation going on.

Possible, but network traffic is prioritised and the fact that they said they applied the IFCS (probably just the tag rather than the whole sheebang) to the missile to fix the issue suggests it isn't just a problem of server tick rate. How would applying IFCS to a missile affect tick rates? There's a lot more stuff needing network bandwidth in Star Citizen than your average game.

Another reason to suggest that some commands are not executed on the client side first. As you say the perceived state by the players - but that's all players and the aim is to try to achieve maximum simultaneity even for clients that are separated by great geographical distance.

Reading my post again I made it sound like all player commands, but it's likely that actions performed that only affect the player directly may execute client side first since it affects orientation and speed (not exactly sure, you can walk around when a server has crashed for example, but you can't open a door or call an elevator, you can't customise a weapon).

Just watching how the game performs you can see that 'change state' commands for doors, elevators, even player item holders, inventories etc that potentially affect all players (because they should see them) not just the one making the command. It wouldn't work out very well if I called an elevator and it's change state wasn't reported to others standing near me in view of that elevator until later. If I loot a crate and it's state isn't updated before the next player loots that crate items could be duplicated, or the player after me gets an inaccurate view of the contents. The ah ha moment when thinking about such things as firing missiles is that they don't 'magic them' just outside the ship, a player inside the ship who is a passenger has to see that missile go up the rail and out the ship, hopefully at the same time as the player making the fire missile command.

If 'full' persistence is to be achieved every single change state has to be recorded to the cache in a timely manner. Obviously with 3.18.0 this wasn't happening. They actually used the words, 'out of sync' when describing the issue.

What players assume is 'lag' in Star Citizen isn't the same type of lag in other multiplayer games.

This 'change of state' is what is recorded on the pCache according to it's parent item - an inventory is still a parent item. The reason we can have attach and detach of components now and not earlier is because of that Persistence Database storing each component and weapons state and attachment. It's the reason another player can watch a player attach and detach that weapon at virtually the same time. If it was 'client first' there would be instances of desync.

Nope. That's not correct. If you'd been following the full discussion up to now you might have questioned that assumption.

The Replication Layer Gateway (it's called a gateway because everything has to go through it) is a system that manages communication between the persistence database, the players and the shards. It has no function to store data. It's a traffic cop, not a scribe so describing it merely as a source doesn't cover it.

just looking at the system map pictogram in the OP should show you, the player clients don't connect directly to server nodes on a shard, they connect to the RL which simultaneously updates the persistence database (pCache, Entity Graph etc) and the server nodes on the shards.The persistence database information has to go through the Replication Layer Gateway to get to the server nodes on the shards.

It's right there, clear as day.

It's not so easy for those who keep comparing Star Citizen to other MMO's to grasp (I know you haven't but the ideas do take root) but there is a big difference between other games and SC regarding how actions are taken in the game - the players don't control anything directly, not even now without the RL. If players understood that they would understand why the game behaves the way it does when it isn't performing at peak. You click something and nothing seems to happen at first, then it does what you expected sometimes after a long wait or you go away and curse the game assuming it just isn't working. Most jump to the conclusion that a long delay after an action is requested by a player means 30K incoming. If that were the case, all player actions would stop for everyone because the server has already died.

In normal multiplayer games run on servers the player initiates an action on the PC client and they see the result of that action immediately, the change is then reported to the game server and the game server then sends that information out to all other connected clients. This is where game lag creeps in.

To deal with lag, a long time ago now, they changed the way player actions are executed and reported to each connected client. The player commands or initiates an action (flying into the ATC zone of a space station initiates an action), but it doesn't happen on the game client on their PC - that requested change state is sent to the DGS. The DGS waits a certain period of time to collect all inputs from player clients (a few milliseconds per cycle), then sends all the results of those collective actions out to all the connected clients at once, the aim being to cut down up/down lag by reducing the potential loss of simultaneity to only the down part of the communication to each client. You command an action and wait, the player stood next to you is watching, and both of you see the commanded action take place at the exact same time - that's the theory. Certain actions in the game are prioritised for this network communication, flight commands, driving commands, player locomotion commands and bullets. Lower down the food chain are cockpit chairs, lights, doors, elevators and of course the lowest of the low - inventories.

There was a bug being reported by players, if you knew why it was happening it was laugh out loud hilarious (to me at least). Players would fire a missile and a command was issued to detach the missile from the ship, the next automatic command for the missile is to start it's flight toward the target. Unfortunately because the 'start flight' command was a lower priority in the network stack so players were flying ahead of the missile and it hit them in the butt when it finally got it's command to fly baby fly!

They said that they had applied the same IFCS system to the missiles to fix the problem, the result of which is that now the priority for the fly baby fly command to the missile was at the same level as the player flight input commands - so no more smacking yourself in the butt... hopefully. Heh heh.

Part of the Replication Layer Gateway's function will be to take that 'collect all inputs' (change state reports) function off the DGS's so that it can simultaneously update the persistence database at the same time as informing the DGS's what actions have been taken by connected game clients. Again to reduce lag because if the players client is updated first, then the DGS's second, and only then the persistence database then the PES system is always going to be significantly lagging behind. Step four of course is that the DGS's are also taking information through the RL to update the game universe regarding things the players do not control (those inputs coming from other systems like the NPC Scheduler - that's why the NPC's are so dozy and slow sometimes, they are way lower on the priority for network traffic than the player) since EVERYTHING is recorded on the persistence database, not just what the players are doing.

The Replication Layer Gateway is a time keeper with a stop watch managing all timestamped information coming in and out from all sources, sorting it, prioritising it and sending it out again to each connected part of the whole system that 'does something' with that data it is managing. It is the drummers beat all other systems follow and synchronise with. It replicates data out to multiple systems simultaneously.

Get it now? Replication (of data to multiple systems)? Layer (information sorted, prioritised coming from the stack of connected services)? Gateway?

I'm replying to my own post here to make a point:

You'll hear some say 'They dumped all the work on iCache! So incompetent!'

When you read this you can shake your head at these poorly informed keyboard worriers because CIG didn't dump iCache, they simply reworked the tool that uses the iCache database to order the data and search function more efficiently and renamed the upgraded iCache to pCache. The application they wrote to do that database management was Entity Graph. No-one would suggest re-ordering the information in a phone book to make it more efficient to search meant having to rebuild the whole telephony system from scratch. They wouldn't have to reinvent the telephone or reinvent the computer that did the searches and made entries to the phone book, they just write a new application for the computer to run that manages the phone book and search queries. Did putting all the parts that make the whole persistence system take longer than they thought? Sure, but that's not exactly new in the world of game development is it?

The process by which the DGS queries Entity Graph to search the pCache database and retrieve information to put items into the game is called Persistent Entity Streaming. When you look at the timeline of the tool building tech milestones (in the OP) what isn't immediately clear is that each item named can be part of a system, once the whole system is named they don't mention the individual items in that system again - they just talk about the whole system. Right now the whole system is PES because the individual parts are done.

No, the database of items in the game was originally called the Item Cache (it was on the DGS originally so it had no persistence when the server spun down) - when they switched to the graph system for storing data about each item they just moved ICache to be remote from the DGS which used the network to query the database, when found to be inefficient they wrote a management app that ran remote to the DGS, they gave the database another name pCache. It's the same thing except the data is organised differently, instead of sequential ordering of hash string identifiers for each item it is now graphed according to parent items. You can see the logic behind the sequence, the servers shutting down destroyed the iCache data so they moved it off the DGS, the app querying it was still on the DGS as part of the game build. When the number of items burgeoned they knew they had to restructure the iCache, so they broke off the app from the game build and wrote a standalone app to manage the database simpler and easier to put it on the same server as the database - voila Entity Graph. taking the app of the dedicated game server has the added benefit of reducing the CPU load for the running dedicated game server.

To do a search query of iCache you'd start at number one and go sequentially, the game grew to millions of items and is expected to increase to billions of items, so sequential item look ups was far too slow and laborious, imagine if they ordered the information in a phone directory by numerical order of the phone number, you'd have to go through every number to see which had the name of the person attached to that number you were trying to find. That would be woefully inefficient you have to search through all the information you don't know to find the information about the person whose name you did know to start off with.

pCache via Entity Graph does it's search queries by parent connection, which means there no 'stop and check' for every single item on the database. Only those items that are connected to one another are search queried, reducing network traffic.

Let me give a real game scenario (very simplified):
We have the new tractor beam coming with attach and detach function. A gun is attached to the entity 'ship' (item 97) at item port number 6, the ship is it's parent item. The ship is on a planet - the planet is it's parent item, the planet is in the Stanton system so the Stanton system (the game universe as it stands) is the planets parent item.

If we still had iCache and that gun had a hash string identifier 900,000 then the old system had to search every item in the game's item database to find the gun. From 1 - 900,000. (this for every single item search query in the game)

With pCache the search starts: game universe identifier (item 01), planet identifier (there's four), ship identifier (maybe a few hundred) on that planet. It doesn't have to search ships that aren't on that planet. Now find the identifier for that specific gun on that ship (maybe between 2 and 24 identifiers to search) and the DGS says it's detaching that gun item from it's parent ship - so that report of your tractor beam activity goes to the database to update that gun items status. It's parent item is about to be changed.

When you use the detach tool it takes that gun with it's hash string identifier removes it from it's parent ship and transfers the gun to the item identifier of wherever you place it - let's say you place on the ground - it's parent item is now the planet (we'll cal it planet with item identifier number 04). So if you log out and back in at a later time and go looking for that gun the DGS taking it's data from pCache says that gun with it's identifier was last attached to the parent planet item number 04 at these coordinates - so search the planet 04 in the pCache database - not the whole item database. Entity Graph is the tool that makes new entries into pCache, deletes entries from the pCache, moves items on the pCache from one parent to another and conducts searches of the pCache.

That's why it's called 'persistence cache' because the static entry for that specific gun item on the database remains even after the DGS has shut down and spun up again. On spin up the DGS queries pCache (using the Entity Graph system) about all the items it should have on that planet (Item 04) and places them where the pCache says they last were attached. So you'll find the gun exactly where you left it.

They're just low poly placeholders for now, when Server Meshing reduces server resource requirements they'll probably replace the low poly models.

Both the rivers and roads need a tool that does two things:

A. Permanently deforms terrain.
C. Clears the area of scattered objects such as rocks and trees.

** It's worth noting that when the 'river guy' first showed the river making tool he also showed how he could deform terrain using a grenade launcher - roads with deep blast pits in them eh?

The river creation tool does both of those things. The level designers have access to a set of pre-made assets which come with the level design tools - trees, rocks of different shapes and sizes, plants etc.

They have chosen to go with rivers first, why? We haven't been told specifically but we can hazard guesses. Roads have to be more level than rivers, you can put rapids and waterfalls for rivers, and their placement cannot be random like rivers there cannot be unevenness when a part of a road connects to another road or to go over a bridge. Roads go over terrain, they don't generally cut through it like rivers. On ramps and off ramps have to be flush to the terrain at their junctions and more accurately aligned than rivers.

Roads have to start somewhere worth going to and end somewhere worth going to otherwise they are not useful, this means they necessarily encroach on areas where there are pre-determined POI's, anyone starting a road, or ending a road has to liaise with the level designers who placed those POI's or even the asset creators who designed and made those POI's. The 3D modellers who made the POI's and Level Designers who place those POI's would have to make entry and exit roads, by hand, that connect seamlessly to the procedurally generated roads.

Roads need 'furniture' like signs, street lights (ship lights are already a vexed issue!), tunnels, bridges and barriers, rivers don't need those things, they generally use the pre-existing planetary tech tool assets like rocks and trees. There can be rivers without bridges, but there can't be long terrain crossing roads without bridges, that would be unrealistic, terrain gradients have to gradual or spanned either overground or underground. There is no way they can do all of that at scale by hand.

Bridges are a lot of complex work in games, because they necessarily have to be 'curvy' which makes them expensive**, especially if you need a way to auto-generate them rather than do it by hand for each one. They will need to design a specific tool to place and align roads, junctions, tunnels and bridges. A road that just hovers above terrain looks terrible, a road that has abrupt boundaries and excessively steep slopes is unusable.

Given the very large distances involved you would also have to consider roadside services for refuelling, repair, habitation and trading etc.

Creating roads with all the work it entails simply for the fun of 'the open road' happily going nowhere isn't really the sort of design decision that's going to get made.

Since there will be an AI NPC population CIG dev's would also have to consider how those assets use the roads.

Prior to importing real world orthographic data other games - such as flight simulators - were plagued by issues which are apparent when close to the ground but good enough from a couple of thousand feet up. There is no 'real world' orthographic data for non-Earth planets. The reason you can't jump into your car and drive to the airport in games such as Microsoft Simulator and XPlane 11, despite decades of iterative development and billions in revenue is because in no small part they haven't cracked the problem of roads.

All in all rivers are the simpler 'add'' for the game right now, roads are more complex. But yes, they have stated many times we will get roads and a first attempt at making roads (short dirt tracks really) has already taken place when they made the planet Hurston.

As an aside - creating a road creation tool that works with spherical grid planets where they are believable and useable is several orders of magnitude more complex than creating the flight model. If CIG could make such a tool - so that those roads and furniture don't have to be hand made - it would be a tool that many other game creation companies would pay good money to license from CIG, especially if they came coupled with the planetary tech tools.

*POI = Point Of Interest. a place to go 'do something'.

** 3D models in games are made of vertices (1 dimensional points in 3D space), lines (2 Dimensional) and faces (2 dimensional). The apparent size of the model is immaterial. A square box is made of 8 vertices, 12 lines (18 if your game engine uses triangles instead of polygons) and 6 faces (12 for triangles) those have to be loaded into Random Access Memory from the hard drive. A sphere that looks smooth with gradual curves so the faces aren't so flat takes hundreds or even thousands of vertices, lines and faces and this is why they are more 'expensive' on system resources.

Any change in level of a road would need to have gradual curves or else if you drove over it you'd notice abrupt changes in the vehicles angle to the flat terrain - again this requires more vertices, lines and faces. If there are hundreds of bridges with on and off ramps, and other on and off ramps or even just hills and bumps on roads that is very expensive on the players PC resources and the game server resources (there's no way roads go in before Server Meshing). It also increases the size of the game download. Because roads have to have collision physics they can't fake these curves, we expect water to be be flat and level, it doesn't have to be bumpy and curvy like a road.

You know.. you can still do the 'buy to melt' with CCU's and concept ships, and you don't need cash to buy from the store unless it's warbond. Your future self might thank you for it.

Yeah.. pCache is a part of a few systems they now describe as Entity Graph, it's not surprising people aren't so familiar with it they only mentioned it once very briefly when they said they did away with iCache and made the Entity Graph system. It's a collective term, Entity Graph is the way the information is written to and read from the database, not just the database itself. If pCache is equivalent to your Windows Registry, Entity Graph is Windows Explorer.

You've got it the wrong way around, iCache was a simple linear sequential look up static database and those look ups took too long so it didn't scale to the size they needed which is why they switched to a graphed database (the type they use in customer service environments) which suits the hierarchical structure of entities in the game - the nested Russian-doll-like Object Container system.

Every single item in the game has a unique hash string identifier attached to it. iCache was arranged for sequential look ups of each item according to it's hash string. The graph database has data organised by it's connection to the topmost item (the parent). So you only need to find the topmost item and run down the tree branches to get to each individual leaf - it's faster than checking all the leaves on the tree, in numerical order, to find the right one you're looking for. The hard copy record of all the leaves is pCache, the way you go up and down the branches to find those leaves, or to make new ones or delete them, is Entity Graph.

EDIT:
iCache = Item Cache database. In very old videos mostly pre OCS introduction you'll hear Chris talk about the Item Cache.
pCache = Persistence Cache database.

When I make posts describing why strange things happen in the game I refer to the 'Persistence Database' to make it easier, there's really not much point describing the system (Entity Graph) in such general posts because all people care about is that there is a search and management system of that database and it works.

There's a couple of things to remember:

We can push the boundaries precisely because we found a development team and said to them.. whatever you need, either time or money, we'll get it to you so that you don' have to limit yourselves or OUR game... and that definitely never happens in other game development. Yep, that's it exactly, the Persistence Cache or pCache (formerly iCache) had to come first and it is needing some 'bedding in'.

Right now every DGS is having to report the change states for all entities in the Stanton system directly to the pCache, on spin up every DGS is having to network query the pCache for the current state of the game universe and stream it into the servers RAM (PES) - there is a lot of replication of data and the network to and from the DGS is not performing adequately to keep the pCache up to date so it is getting out of sync, it's connected to a network of separated independently running DGS's that are spinning up and down all the time firing in to the pCache data about multiple Stanton systems - the pCache database has been running continuously since 3.18.0 went to Live.

The Replication Layer Gateway will get between the DGS's and pCache and record a continuous timed snapshot of the current state of the Live verse (a still image in time in a picture frame if you will) but without the overhead of holding all the Stanton entities and players ie running a live game which is updated from the pCache. The reset that is coming is to re-set the pCache to 'midnight' again as it were - wipe the image clean and start drawing it again. Separating the Replication Layer to an expandable system will allow that 'up' and 'down' data to be spread over more servers each with their own network bandwidth scaled to not get overloaded.

The game universe time is synchronised to the hundredths of a second across all running DGS's. Each entity change state report has a timestamp.

This change state idea is an important one to grasp. Every entity in the game - a ships gun, the turret, the doors, the doors of the hangars, the elevators and even individual bullets - are all entities that can do something, their state can change. The pCache records a static image of all the entities (it's just a static database to query) - for arguments sake let's say every 10 milliseconds - each DGS is sending data about the current state of every entity to the Pcache as fast as it can, and it's also querying the pCache about each entity recorded on it to update it's view of the current game universe all while running the game for the connected clients. The pCache is not dynamic in the sense that changes happen on it, it's like a video stream where each frozen in time frame is recorded. The Replication Layer doesn't run the game universe for clients and it can decide if data is being replicated (redundant unchanged states of entities), or if it's out of date, from each DGS and streamline the data going to and from the pCache, now each DGS doesn't have to decide the difference between 'currently reported by DGS' state of each entity and compare it to the state of that entity in the pCache - it's change state - the RL does it. The Replication Layer currently isn't taking network traffic from each connected client - the players PC - like the DGS has to, because it doesn't exist but when it does it will take that network traffic off the DGS's. The player client input goes to the RL gets timestamp sorted and prioritised and sent to the pCache, the pCache data is collected by the DGS to update the game entities.

It still won't be optimal though until each DGS is only holding a part of the Stanton system - ie meshing. Once SM goes in the amount of network traffic each DGS generates will reduce significantly, and the DGS mesh can be expandable as needed to maintain a lower network output per DGS.

Basically they couldn't both build the block systems and put them in place, then expand the backend and the frontend at the same time, one had to be done first - so it was the backend. pCache goes in first and gets tested (3.18.0), then the Replication Layer and it will be tested, then Server Meshing.

Server Meshing is splitting up the game build that each server holds so that each DGS holds just a part of the whole game universe, in the first instance it will be static so that each DGS will only get a part of the game universe and it can't expand beyond that part, in practise it's game universe borders are fixed. Dynamic SM means that any DGS server could scale up to the full game universe as player loads demand as well as splitting the game universe over several 'expandable' sections of the verse, it streams out what players leave behind and streams in where the players are going (almost exactly like SSOCS) - as Chris said Static Server Meshing the easier part.

Regarding the naysayers.. they said OCS / SSOCS wouldn't happen or be a disaster too, at the time.

They'll get it to work because they always do, they have a plan. Since no-one has ever tried something like this before there are bound to be wrinkles that are unforeseen... but we CAN see the pCache (Persistence) is working which is pretty neat because the system they used to build the pCache originally came from the business world of customer databases, they adapted this business database type structure after the iCache didn't perform as they needed. That's what they do, they look around and grab solutions no-one else would think to try.

That is the plan for the Reliant MAKO News Van, for players to be able to make and stream video content inside the game, to roleplay being a news reporter. There is no 'faster than light' communication in the game. You need to record and edit footage then send it to the comms satellites to send that video to other star systems, local can be livestreamed probably.

If you want to see the News Van editing suite being used it's in the Jax McCleary video where he goes to save Jimmy doing a solo attack on the Hammerhead. Jax is talking to someone using that editing suite.

That's what this project is about - making it all possible. That's why they spend much more time than any other game developer making the tools to make it possible.

You are like most looking at the current Star Citizen alpha and thinking this is substantially what the Star Citizen universe is to become, maybe with a few more places and player professions added - it isn't even close. The current Alpha is a tiny test-bed of features they want to test at scale.

Full Persistence - why? Why bother with all the trouble it brings like gowns and ship wrecks littering the place?
It's because the final game will have a universe simulated (as realistically as they can make it) that runs permanently whether there are any players around to see it or not. Hardly anyone has noticed, or commented on, that the persistence database has been running continuously since 3.18.0 went live - that is why it needs to be reset because as in all things the first run is never perfect. That is the plan, to have an endlessly running universe simulator so that there can be dynamic populations, a dynamic economy, a dynamic schedule of events, natural disasters and wars and pandemics. Places will boom and bust, things will age, some things will be destroyed and some things will be built, there will be mass migrations of populations, tourism that both the NPC AI and players can engage in - it is not going to be static like other games.

The servers are designed to run inter-connected cloud services, that's why they use AWS rather than the simpler backbone that other multiplayer games use. What would be the point of doing the R&D for things like Object Container Streaming, Quantum, Quasar, the DNA system if Cloud Imperium were simply taking the same approach as other games? There wouldn't be any point.

People see things going on on the RSI website like lore posts, little snippets of shows like Empire Report, Imperator elections, faction fighting, The Spectrum - they think it is just 'lore flavour' like other games. They think things like whale watching on Orison, Murray Cup Racing, Invictus Launch Week, Intergalactic Aerospace Expo, the Hadesian Artifact are just scripted one off events a McGuffin for a mission that has no other meaning or effect once completed, or made up modules that only players will experience and that don't connect to the daily life of this permanently running 'Living Breathing Universe'.

Star Citizen and Squadron 42 are about players being able to create their own unique character and story arc and live the life of that character in an ever evolving dynamic universe.

Players will log into the game, as they do now and they will do their stuff - but in the future a big part of the Star Citizen experience will be FOMO - because there's always something going on somewhere in the game universe every minute of every single day and you can watch it, read about it and follow it all on The Spectrum. You can contribute to The Spectrum using equipment like the Reliant MAKO News Van. Why would a game that is only for dog-fighting and trading have a ship equipped with cameras and an editing suite? Why is it that people who follow the game's development don't even ask the obvious questions about things like this? Why on Earth would they spend so much time and money on 'lore flavour' before the game is even close to being ready for primetime?

You'll be able to follow all the events going on in this living breathing universe on other platforms like mobiles and streaming media.
