When a server holds only a small part of the Stanton system (or other planetary system) then it's network bandwidth requirement, and the CPU/RAM required to produce/receive that network traffic data is significantly reduced.

The persistence database has been running continuously since 3.18.0 went live, because of network issues it has been getting more and more out of sync with what the players are doing on the live servers.

They have to find the network issues and reset the corrupted database which is causing all the in-game issues. Anymore network issues that show up after the reset to zero then have to be dealt with and once they are they'll reset to start the PES database clock running again and it will continue running permanently unless there is another reset needed.

Well it's great you link to all the Issue Council bugs.. but.. these are all PES related, every one of them.

Let's start with an analogy. Someone takes their car to the mechanic because it's not working as they expect. They turn it on and put it in gear (drive), they don't know how an internal combustion engine works they just know how to use it.
The driver leaves the car with the mechanic and comes back and says..

Driver: The car just keeps stalling
Mechanic: Yes, because you had a partial blockage in the radiator hose.
Driver: The engine temperature light keeps coming on.
Mechanic: Yes, because you had a partial blocakge in the radiator hose.
Driver: The oil warning light kept coming on.
Mechanic: Yes, because you had a partial blockage in the radiator hose.

Driver: Have you fixed the stalling issue?
Mechanic:Yes, we've replaced the radiator hose.
Driver: Have you fixed the engine temperature issue?
Mechanic: Yes, we've replaced the radiator hose.
Driver: Have you fixed what's causing the oil warning light to come on?
Mechanic: Yes, we replaced the radiator hose.

When you understand how the game works, and how PES touches absolutely everything in the game all of these bugs make sense, they're all part of the same problem. They have said they are resetting these databases because they have become out of synch with what the players are doing in the game universe, of course all anyone cares about is the 'wipe' is about reputation and aUEC, but it isn't.

PES is like a video camera, it stores snapshot images over time of where everything, literally everything, in the game is and what it's current state is. Because of slow network things get out of synch. The video frames are late, jumbled up or even missing completely - the video becomes unwatchable.

The PES database was started running the second 3.18.0 went to live - and it never stops... until the reset for 3.18.1 hits the live servers.

This is the way the game currently works:
Server spins up to level designer made static state, before any player is logged in the server state is updated by the PES registry to current universe state --->You initiate a 'change state' for some entity on your game client ----> entity change state registers on PES registry ---> PES registry updates all connected game clients about the state of all entities in the game universe. Any late registry entry, or even missed registry entry, is going to make everything go wonky and appear to do really wacky stuff.

When testing the PTU for example I saw the bug where you drag something from a loot box and it appears to go into your personal inventory then after about a half a second (or longer) it disappears from your inventory and re-appears back in the loot box, if you waited - sometimes up to a few minutes you see the reverse happen, the item disappears from the loot box and appears in the personal inventory. This is because your game client let's you see your initial action, but it hasn't updated the persistence database yet, so the game is then updated by PES but the change you made hasn't reached the the PES database yet, the game is getting an 'out of date update' - it's out of synch. After some time your queue for registering that loot item on PES , the 'state change' of the loot item, registers with PES and now the action you took registers in the game. Or, for some reason the network message never got through either on the 'up' to PES, or the 'down' from PES to your game client.

For someone who is dragging items out of a loot box, or from local inventory to ship inventory, then immediately moving on they think, the items did go into my inventory but then disappeared - of course they can't see that it went back to the loot box or local inventory for a time. So they say.. 'it just disappeared'. Or it may be the 'down' PES message doesn't get through, so again the inventory is not updated that item appears 'gone'.

You think, I ordered the hangar doors opened and on my game client those hangar doors are open - but that change state hasn't reached the PES database yet and so it thinks the doors are still closed. You fly toward the doors and explode because the game persistence registry says.. That ship just crashed into closed doors according to the information I have received. You think 'I landed on the pad and then randomly blew up', the persistence registry doesn't know you reached the pad, it thinks you blew up at the doors, so the next time the PES registry updates the game it just says - the ship exploded. Even if you stored your ship, the last persistence registry entry that 'got there' says - no.. that ship was completely damaged, so the next time it gets loaded into the game and the PES registry is queried for the state of that ship... Boom! Exactly the same with the location of your ship, the PES registry has your ship back home even though you flew it and even stored it somewhere else during your last play session.

People say..Why is CIG wiping credits from my wallet? Or.. Why is CIG resetting my bounty hunter status / rep status? They aren't, the money in your wallet and bounty hunter status you got during your last play session never made it to the persistence database before you logged off. So next time you log on and the persistence database is queried it gives your game client the last information it had, it doesn't know what you did for part of your last play session.

That's why they are resetting the databases, that's why there has to be a complete full wipe, when a server first spins up it queries the PES registry to ask - where is everything and what is it's current state?'It's not like the old days when everything started from a base setting. - the first reset is a test to see if the network issues are resolved, and the next reset to is a return to starting point once they dealt with any issues that arose from the first test reset. Remember the PES database has been running continuously, getting more and more out of synch, since 3.18.0 went live. It's like winding the clock back to midnight and letting it run.. see if it's on time, if it isn't you try a fix then you wind it back to midnight and let it run again.

Everyone thinks 'they're just ignoring all the IC bugs!' - no they aren't - they know what's causing them so there's no point in addressing them individually, and in their last TWISC they say they are fixing those persistence database network issues... so those bugs will all go away, unless they have another cause.

I think the plan is that they allow you to have the controls to make it as simple or complex as you wish. As I said you can play with the individual thruster settings to get the effect you want right now, it's just that there's no way currently to save those settings and re-apply them. I'm sure in the future there will be.

It's also the case that they aren't exactly fastidious about updating the Roadmap Companion when changes are made to teams activity.

The narrative team do pick up work (and extra dialogue for non-story peripheral NPC's etc) for Squadron 42 even after all the main story has been fixed, the narrative team also do work on PU missions - so they will always be doing work as long as the PU is having content added.

This is misleading, I understand why you make that statement but it is not the case.

If the Magnus system is in a chapter of Squadron 42 then only work on that chapter will be represented on the Progress Tracker, not work on a specific location or asset in Squadron 42. We can look at the teams assigned and surmise what that content in the chapter listing might be, we can cross reference that with the monthly reports. According to the Progress Tracker the only team working on any chapter of Squadron 42 is the Vehicle Feature Team - we know that isn't the case, because they aren't the only ones mentioned in Squadron 42 monthly reports, and there is nothing in Squadron 42 that isn't in a chapter.

The Progress Tracker also lists items where teams are only working part time on a feature, and so it is (deliberately?) obscured exactly how many are working on that feature and for how long, because if there is 1 days work on April 1st, and another 2 days work on September 30th and 31st it is still represented as 6 months continuous part-time assigned work for an upstream team. The EUPU team was assigned anti-gravity for 53 weeks, but because it is marked as pat time we have no idea what proportion of that 53 weeks they actually worked on it, or what proportion of the EUPU team did the actual work. The Progress Tracker was carefully designed to only give the information they wish to give... which is why the old one was discontinued.

We also have been told that not everything is put onto the Progress Tracker, and there are items on the Progress Tracker that are listed as 'unannounced' with a short description and the number of teams working on that unannounced feature. Because they know we scrutinise everything and are perfectly capable of extrapolating from information given, to avoid giving information about an item they simply won't add that item or feature to the Progress Tracker. In monthly reports they state specifically they are still continuing work on the Pyro system, but that monthly-report-mentioned specific work is not represented on the Progress Tracker. Also mentioned in monthly reports and the last SC Live is continuing work on the AI, yet the Progress Tracker says all assigned work on AI has ceased since the middle of February.

The idea that simply because information is not shown on the Progress Tracker means there is no work being done is erroneous. We are given multiple sources of information regarding work done. They are very careful to curate and cross reference what information is put on the Progress Tracker to avoid spoilers. it is not simply a case of grabbing all the JIRA's and dumping that information onto the Progress Tracker when they get around to it, they do carefully filter what they put on the Progress Tracker, which is very probably why it is often updated late.

I think it is also worth pointing out that as Squadron 42 becomes feature complete less and less information is going to go on the Progress Tracker about it. It is also the case that when they mention work on Squadron 42 features we have no idea at what stage that work is, if it's work flagged by QA to be re-looked at, they can still say work is ongoing on that feature. They have no requirement to state in any communication - the only reason that feature has landed back on our desks is because QA play-tested it and found issues.

There were 34 teams assigned to 'Bug Fixing and Tech Debt' and 'Miscellaneous Support' over last year which is really a non-spoiler catch-all term for widespread QA testing and any work that results from it - expect those two Progress Tracker items to be filled out in the next update for the block 6 or 12 months because those two items will remain even after a full release, and there is no way for us, or even them, to know how long that process will last.

It depends on what the bottle-neck in each individual PC system is. If you have a multi-core CPU which is currently being under-utilised by the game then you will see improvement initially, for example I have a Ryzen 3900X with a GTX 1080Ti, the CPU is running around 50% or less while the GPU is at 100%. So Vulkan will improve my CPU performance, but not so much the GPU. If you have a CPU and GPU that are maxed out you might see some performance gain initially, but it's not likely to be dramatic and when they up the workload (as expected) then the same limitations of the hardware will be apparent.

The biggest limiting factor is VRAM currently, anything less than 8GB will struggle with or without Vulkan. That's why 32GB RAM is recommended by the players because some of the VRAM load is taken up by RAM.

Gravity is accurately modelled in SC, if you hover above a planet and turn off your engines you WILL eventually crash down to the surface... I know I've tried it. The thrusters in Star Citizen are not the rocket engines of Flight of Nova, they are so powerful that they can easily kill you and they can fairly easily take you out of a gravity well formed by a planetary body, they need to do that because dog-fighting in space requires such powerful thrusters, without which any space manoeuvring would be long and tedious. You'll notice the section of the video where the player docks with the space station is speeded up - and people complain about the 3 or 4 minutes train or shuttle ride in Star Citizen - if you took 10 or 12 minutes just to dock with every space station how would the average player react to that? Even spacecraft now have computers to do those positioning and docking manoeuvres, exactly like the IFCS system the primary goal of those flight computers is to keep the humans alive at the end of it.

When comparing Star Citizen to games like KSP the thing they forget is how many times you die just doing manoeuvres in KSP.

The thing that stops you going into orbit in SC are the automatic 'station keeping' systems of the IFCS (if you go to external view in SC you'll see the manoeuvring thrusters are always firing even without inputs, if you run out of hydrogen fuel or have disabled thrusters you will crash and burn), currently the only way to turn off all IFCS functions is to completely power down, presumably there will be other options in the future, even taking out the computers altogether from their component bays.

You can set individual power limits and thrust levels via the MFD's in your ship, and you'll see how it affects your flight characteristics, but basically no-one does - why would you gimp yourself if you don't have to? Those who complain about the way ships handle, why aren't you making videos of you trying out different thruster settings? The dev's who set flight characteristics have said they only use the same settings the players have access to. It's very complex to get the balance right for all scenarios, THAT'S WHY we have such a sophisticated IFCS system. If you want to spend dozens of hours tweaking thruster settings, only to have to redo all of them because you had to reclaim the ship when you f'up - have at it. If you choose thruster settings that give a pleasing looking 'with inertia' flight at Daymar, you better not go near Hurston because if you do you'll drop like a stone.

Speeds are limited in Star Citizen only because they have accurately modelled G force effects on the player, and so again unlike in the OP's video, to limit G force excesses on the human body they limit max velocities, it's a safety limit imposed by the IFCS, not an artificial limit. Sure you could go up to 4000m/s but then you'd have to spend 20 minutes or so doing retro burns that didn't kill you. You can do retro burns from very high speeds in Star Citizen, but you won't see much on screen because it simulates blackout and red-out. The alternative is to have you doing retro burns for many minutes at a time to avoid such high G forces. Star Citizen does have fully modelled and accurate Newtonian physics, but unlike FoN and KSP those Newtonian physics apply to EVERYTHING IN THE GAME not just the ships.

When he says that it looks great that things fly off your ship if you bump something, yeah nice, but it didn't affect the way the ship handled did it? There were no 'Major Torque Imbalance' red warnings on the screen.

What people say they want and the results of what they actually want are not the same things. I know people don't like to hear it but the flight model is still a work in progress, and it works well enough right now to test other things in the game. We know, because they have told us, that they have worked on a different flight model in Squadron 42 which will come to the PU eventually. The servers are at capacity with what they have now, adding the extra complexity of orbital trajectories for space stations, planets and moons IS possible, but not a priority.

It's worth pointing out that in the OP's video the space station was in the equivalent of LEO, it wasn't in geostationary orbit where even with fully modelled physics the planet absolutely would look 'unmoving' below you - that what's geostationary means. In Low Earth Orbit the International Space Station has to keep making thruster burns to maintain orbit because of atmospheric drag, that same amtospheric drag is in Star Citizen.

They have shown us that the improved flight model will accurately model aerodynamic forces on ships in atmosphere, not just one atmosphere and gravity, but all the different ones in the game. KSP doesn't have that.

Everything in the video is possible in Star Citizen, that game doesn't have the complex IFCS system that Star Citizen has, and that game doesn't accurately model the G force effects on the player from I can see in the video provided.. and here's one reason why.. in that game you can't leave the cockpit and go walking around inside your ship while all those maneouvres are taking place, there are no passengers, there is no physicalised cargo or other items to go flying around inside your ship... but CIG do plan to model this anything not secured in your ship will react to changes in velocity. If you've left the computer flying the ship while you go watch a screen in the mess hall you'd better hope that IFCS computer is sophisticated enough not to kill you while you're sucking on a burrito. Is there zero gravity aboard ships in Flight of Nova? You don't know, because you can't leave the cockpit, whereas there definitely will be gravity generation and zero gravity (microgravity) in Star Citizen.

I'm sure Blackmaze knows about gravity and effects, and has a basic grasp of orbital mechanics perhaps, however the extension from what he knows to what he can surmise with critical thinking skills about the differences in one game and the other may need a little longer to sink in. Star Citizen is different, with respect, the simple model this lone designer has come up with looks more accurate precisely because his game doesn't have the complexity of Star Citizen. People think the flight model and physics isn't accurate when actually it's main issue (for some players) is that it's TOO accurate, they have to have a 29th Century IFCS to cope with it. My feeling is that there should be older ships in the game without such a sophisticated IFCS so that players who want to play a Flight of Nova, KSP type game can - and those that don't can stick with the 29th Century IFCS equipped ships. We also need, and will get, custom settings we can save for ship configurations - which could become a part of the information trading. you go to a star system and buy the custom settings for each planetary body for the type of ship you're flying.

We are very fortunate to have someone of Tony Zurovec's vision and experience. If Tony has a weak point I'd say that it is that he doesn't take into account, or doesn't have the time to take into account, that not everyone might be fully up to speed with all the output from Cloud Imperium.

To understand what Tony is talking about when he talks of his plans regarding creating this 'Living Breathing Universe' you have to remember how the game is placed on the game servers. There are rules which we have to remember because they are unavoidable and unbreakable.

These are the rules:
Virtual NPC's and Instantiated NPC's can only 'live' in server RAM on the Live Game servers - without which they would simply 'stop' as static data on a hard drive (as they do now), you wouldn't see them in the game if they aren't loaded into Dedicated Game Server - Random Access Memory. No record of them could be sent to the persistence database.

Quanta live their 'lives' in the RAM of the servers running the Quantum simulation because not all the games locations will be loaded into RAM on Live DGS servers all the time.

The theory:
Both the Live Game and Quantum simulation are updated via the persistence database. When a Quanta's location in the game universe is placed on a Live Game server, in a place where either a player or NPC would expect to see them, they are logged out of the Quantum simulation and in to the Live game and loaded into RAM.

When that location where the NPC is in the Live Game is removed from a server the NPC is logged back into the Quantum simulation to carry on it's life, all the information about that NPC was sent to the persistence databases and when that NPC goes back to the Quantum simulation the information from the persistence database is used to update that NPC on the Quantum simulation.

In practise:
There is a pirate base called Spider - one of the perks of subscribing was to name and give a back-story to a pirate character on Spider, but that ceased a while ago because there were so many who qualified to do it.

If no player is near Spider then Spider is not loaded onto any game server, there is nowhere for them to put NPC's either 'real' or virtual there are no probability volumes for them to encounter. That is what the Quantum simulation is for. If a player is near Spider but not in a place where they could see NPC's, ie Spider is loaded onto a Live Game server - then it is populated by virtual NPC's which came from the Quantum simulation, they interact with the Live Game systems like probability volumes, shops and ATC. Once the player is somewhere they can see an NPC that virtual NPC is instantiated, they are now 'real'. When the player leaves so that he no longers sees any NPC's, the instantiated NPC's go back to being virtual NPC's, and if the server with Spider on it spins down, the NPC's go back to the part of the Quantum simulation where Spider is - to continue their lives until the next time a Live Game server spins up with Spider on it.


Laying it out:

There are the Quanta which are AI entities that exist in a virtual simulation real time copy of the Live Game universe. They have their names and back-story, they have personality traits, faction affiliations and goals. They have a history log of all the things they have done and places they've been... basically they live out a life in a virtual copy of the game universe that runs in parallel with the Live Game universe.

Then there are virtual NPC's, they only exist in the Live Game universe but they aren't physicalised unless a player is in their vicinity to encounter them. You'll see them show up on long range scanners for example, they do all the same things the Quanta do except now they are in the Live Game on a spun up server's RAM.

Instantiated NPC's are those you can see and physically react to or interact with, again they have all the same traits as Quanta and virtual NPC's but they also have an appearance (DNA somewhat randomly chosen just like in the character creator), dress style and where appropriate a vehicle etc.

The key thing to remember is that Quanta can become virtual NPC's in the Live Game, and virtual NPC's can become instantiated as 'real' NPC's, then back again through the same process in reverse back to Quanta. They still retain all the information, personal belongings and history log because of the persistence database.

If you remember TZ talks about a Quanta character (Draygon?) who is spawned into the game as a virtual NPC when a virtual NPC is in a place to encounter him. As a virtual NPC he goes to the space station bar where he would be instantiated if a player was there to see him, he then leaves the bar and goes back to being a virtual NPC (if he was instantiated) and then he's sent back to being a Quanta running on the Quantum simulation. If you take the bounty on Draygon, then when you are in a location where you could possibly encounter him (ie that location exists on a Live DGS server) then Draygon is brought back from the Quantum simulation to the Live game.

If you are in a location (ie a Live DGS server has spun up that location) where you would meet an Quanta NPC character again it will be the same character.. and they will remember you... because that Quantum simulation is a real time virtual copy of the real game universe taking it's information from the real time persistence database. You could call it a shadow universe which is much cheaper to run millions of NPC's on because they aren't physicalised, there are no game assets taking up RAM in that simulation, and they don't use the live game facilities like ATC.

If NPC's were created 'as required' and then burned, there would be no need for the Quantum simulation at all.

If there was no such thing as Server Meshing, ie all the game locations were running on Live servers all the time then there would be no need for a Quantum simulation, which is why there is no more information to be had about the Quantum simulation until Server Meshing goes into the game. We've been told what it is, what it does and why it needs to be there.

The Quantum simulation provides population persistence of 'permanent characters' without the overhead of placing them in the game permanently because once they are streamed out of DGS RAM and off the hard drive when a server spins down, they would be gone (paused) without the persistence database. None of the NPC's lives are paused or deleted just because there are no players around to see them or simply because no server has spun up with their location on it. They are all living their eventful lives in the RAM of the servers running the Quantum simulation until they are called back to the Live Game environment.

I've read people saying that other games have populations of 'good' NPC's, but I don't know of any game where they would run a virtual parallel simulation of that game in real time just so that the players could encounter an endless number of ever evolving NPC's with whom they can have a permanent history any time they encounter them.

I'm not a fanboy of anyone, but to me the Quantum simulation is an almost genius solution to the problems that spinning up and spinning down game servers brings to the 'life' of the characters the players would encounter in this Living Breathing Universe. For that we should thank the team involved in giving it to us. So yeah, let's see him, and them, on an SC Live.

They aren't running a finished game, they are testing a work in progress so they must do what they need to, to do those tests. They have varied player numbers (they went from 50 to 100 then briefly 130 then back to 100), and they didn't choose to reduce player numbers despite the difficulties the current live build has, of which they are well aware - that should tell you something.

Presumably, bearing in mind they are making choices, they know that reducing player numbers would either:

Not make a heap of difference anyway (which is what they've actually said), so presumably their tests are showing that the current 3.18 issue isn't related to player load.

Yes, I do know that.. it's the reason for server meshing. So bearing in mind SM takes away that problem then player numbers per server are not affected by that problem.

There's no point in reducing player numbers in the current build because stress testing and observing player behaviours IS the aim of the current builds.

I'm only saying what the developers have said when asked about the increase in player count. Specifically Benoit Bausejour.

Also you're forgetting how Server Side Object Container Steaming works, and this was explained by Chris himself. When players group together in one small area this is the best case scenario from the point of view of server performance because only the area the players are in is streamed into RAM on the server. The worse case scenario is when players spread out all over the Stanton system because now the DGS has to stream all assets for those areas into RAM, and run all the logic for those areas - NPC's etc, transports etc.

So you see it's not that the players are a particularly heavy load, its that more players are more likely to spread out around the Stanton system, in terms of server load one player can bring a whole planet into server RAM. This is the reason for Server Meshing.

The players are only a small fraction of the server load, players have a greater effect on the backend services - that's what the problem with 3.18.0 was - and it doesn't matter how many servers the players are on, they are still the same number of players stressing the backend services.

The servers running at max capacity allows them to see where they can make improvements to performance now, so that when server meshing comes they have an idea of how to still make most efficient use of game servers even though those servers have a significantly reduced workload because they aren't loading the whole of the Stanton system onto one server.They are still building and testing the game, there's not much point in taking the easy route when the thing they want to test is how the game mechanics run under load.

So, no reducing player counts wouldn't help them achieve what they want to achieve, and wouldn't even improve the players experience much either. They did run 3.17.X at 130 players per server, it didn't affect performance worth noticing.

If all you want to do is sight-see then the PTU is perfect for you, the EPTU is smooth as butter. If renaming your Live folder to EPTU is too much bother then it's a valid thing to as: is a game in alpha really your cup of tea?

You bumped into the ship because you didn't position yourself in the entrance of the ship properly. Your automatic EVA systems try to re-orientate you to the local grid of the ship, the thing is that re-orientation is crude, it orientates to the 0,0,0 coordinates of the grid and you don't realise you are spun 358 degrees to the ships grid so you think you're relatively upright with respect to the floor, your EVA system says no you need to spin 358 degrees left to get to 0.0.0 orientation - which of course you try to fight because you weren't expecting it to do that. The way to avoid it happening is to pause just as you enter the ship and let the auto system orientate you before proceeding.

There is a new EVA system coming, it was discussed by Chris at the last Digital CitizenCon. It is being tested in Squadron 42 first.

You should watch the last ISC where they discussed server meshing with Benoit Bausejour.

There is no replication of player owned objects across shards, they have said that in the case of player built bases / houses they will replicate them across shards but they would be locked (no-one can enter or use them) on all shards the player isn't on.

There is the concept of stowed and un-stowed. Anything stowed is player account bound, it goes where you go, anything in your inventory, on your ship or in your own hab / hangar is stowed. Anything un-stowed after you leave the game or a shard can be taken by another player, which they can keep if they stow it in their account.

When you log into a shard they will try to put you on the shard you last went on, if you go to another shard any items you own will move with you to the new shard... off the other shard you were on when there is no-one there to see it happen if it is un-stowed, if there is someone there to see it happen then it remains on that shard until no-one is around. Any un-stowed items (things you leave around the place) will stay on the shard where you left them, they won't go to any other shard. If you left a ship on a shard and move to another shard and there are players around to see it you'll have to do an insurance claim for that ship. If you left anything else on another shard un-stowed - you've lost it until they devise a way to insure player owned gear.

Again, there is no replication of player owned items across shards. Anything you leave un-stowed belongs to the game universe now, it no longer belongs to you.

Everything is physicalised including bullets in the gun. The bullet moves from the magazine, down the barrel and toward the target affected by atmospheric density, gravity and wind, you can have bullet jam. You will be able to look in a different direction than the aim point of a weapon because the weapon aim point is not tied to your current view, unlike other FPS games. When you look down a sight your eye and head move to the gun sight. We will have magazine bullet stripping and refilling 'by hand' as well as manual scope sighting.

The current NPC population are the fixed NPC's, ie they have roles specific to where they are - they are handled by something called the NPC scheduler. They still have 24 hour lives, they can go off duty, sleep, go eat, go to bars, go shopping, go sight-seeing, go on holiday etc. They will have unique back stories, personalities and appearance eventually. The uses something called DNA to randomly create millions of unique looking NPC's. It's the same tech we get to use in character creation.

In the future there will be a dynamic group of NPC's (far more of them) that can roam around the game universe at will just like players do. They will have unique appearances (all different), unique personalities, allegiances and back stories, preferences and goals to achieve, they will react to events in the game universe dynamically. These are called Quanta. Some will have their own ships but there is also regularly scheduled public transport anyone can use.

The game universe will have a calendar of events, like the Murray Cup Racing Championship for example, Invictus Launch Week and the Intergalactic Aerospace Expo - but there are more coming, there will be politics and elections. There will be factions both political and lawless.

The Spectrum in the game is like the internet in the real world, you'll be able to do anything on Spectrum you can do on the internet. Watch sports going on in the verse, online shopping, video calling, TV shows like Galactic Gear and Whitley's Guides, and the news such as Empire Report (You'll see the Spectrum first in Squadron 42). Players will also be able to stream in-game video onto the Spectrum where they can roleplay news reporters with ships like the Reliant MAKO News Van. There will be natural disasters, pandemics, boom and bust economics, gold rushes, faction fighting and wars.

Squadron 42 takes place in the same game universe as Star Citizen except it is in the recent past. The character you create in Squadron 42 can retire to the PU (as ex-military) with all the reputation, history log and assets / achievements you gained in SQ42 going with you to the PU. Some of the same characters, or those related to those characters will also be in the PU and they will recognise / know you. Your military training and experience will unlock missions, equipment and vehicles and places in the PU closed to non-military. *Lore - not law.

There are 3 tiers of social status in the UEE, in the game universe:

Those with no legal status in the UEE, from non-aligned star systems. There is also slavery and indentured servitude outside of the UEE.

Civilians - pay more taxes, can only vote in local elections, are not allowed to own businesses that are strategic to the UEE.

Citizens - pay less tax, can stand as candidates in UEE Senatorial elections, can vote in all elections, can own production nodes (factories) and businesses essential to the UEE. Citizenship has to be earned either by service to the UEE (Joining the military in Squadron 42 for example), or you can do work for UEE agencies like the Advocacy, or you can buy citizenship - but it's expensive. No-one can gain ex-military status in the PU except by starting Squadron 42.

The Persistent universe is going to be a living breathing universe where everything dynamically changes over time with things going on all the time. No one player will ever be able to see it all. It is planned that there will be apps that connect into the game so that you can do things, and watch things in the game while not logged in to play, on mobiles for example.

With Nanite you still have to create the 3D meshes before you can convert them, which means developer time. Someone has to create those in-game assets. They have been using photogrammetry to cut down on 3D modelling time in 3D editors, but that only works for static objects like landscape items or background non-interactable items - any object that 'does stuff' cannot be made with photogrammetry (or laser scanning) alone it has to be produced by hand by 3D artist developers.

The procedural tech developed by CIG uses 3D mesh building blocks with all the 'does stuff' (doors, lights, switches, panels, display screens etc) included with each module to create larger in-game assets. Someone still has to create those 3D meshes before the procedural tools can arrange them into larger units. With the sheer volume of assets that will go into the PU it would take an inordinate amount of time and money to handcraft every single asset individually and their planet tech tools already generate landscapes, and even biomes, more efficiently than imported photogrammetry would allow.

About 3D meshes
3D models are made of vertices, edges and faces all with positions (coordinates) relative to a single point within the mesh bounding box (the X=0, Y=0, Z=0 point). The more of these the higher the number of megabytes per model which affects both download size, time to load into RAM, amount of RAM used per model and the level of apparent detail of a model. The size of a model is immaterial, a very tiny 3D model with extremely high detail is a larger file size than a very big 3D model with fewer vertices, edges and faces. The aim in game development is to use the smallest file sized models as you can. Photogrammetry generally produces very high detailed models with a file size far too big for general in-game use so those models produced by photogrammetry have to be simplified by reducing the numbers of vertices, edges and faces. 3D mesh editors can do this automagically for you.

In order to put detail back onto a simplified 3D model there are two techniques that 'fake detail' on a 3D model surface relevant to this discussion, one is called Physically Based Rendering (PBR) coupled with height maps which is what CIG uses and now of course there is also Nanite in UE5 which replaces the vertices, edges and faces on very highly detailed meshes altogether.

Nanite cannot be used on models with skeleton rigs or splines ie nothing that changes shape - like a tree branch in the wind, a field of crops swaying the wind or when touched, a flag or hair. As far as I'm aware you cannot make Nanite 'models' destructible / deformable, translucent or light emitting, whereas you can with traditional 3D meshes, in fact CIG have mentioned they intend to have deformable 3D models in the game. Nanite also has issues with 3D models that are closely stacked - which is of course what all non-terrain, non-structural Star Citizen assets are made of, closely grouped entities. PBR / height maps are applied to the (simple) 3D model faces to create 'apparent detail' on the surface, historically it had the disadvantage that any surface detail created by PBR / height maps didn't have collision physics, however CIG created a system (Thank Evo Herzeg!) so that PBR / height map generated detail does have collision physics in Star Citizen and Squadron 42.

Specifically about NMS.
Procedural generation can take place either in an external 3D mesh editor like Houdini, Maya, Sculptris, Blender etc or in the game engine. With NMS the planetary assets are procedurally generated according to a fixed formula in the game using voxels, whereas CIG uses procedural tools in the editors to produce fixed assets (the industry term for this is 'baked in' assets) which are then exported from the editor and imported into the game where they are 'hand placed' by level editors.. they do not change once exported from the editors. What CIG are doing with the tools they have developed is procedural production, not in-game procedural generation.

The OP's logic is flawed, they don't spin up low population density servers in any region if they can avoid it - which they can. If occasionally you find yourself on a low pop server it's either one of two reasons:

Either the server has just spun up and players are being gradually added.

or

Those already on the server are logging out... probably due to poor performance or connection issues.

It isn't cost effective to run low population servers... in any region.

They are controlled by the NPC scheduler service currently, however they require server core threads to run the AI behaviours. Those server cores run all the logic and physics for all entities in the game - typically around 100,000 or more entities. Worse, until the full Vulkan / Gen12 implementation the physics for all entities on a server run on one thread. An entity is any object or collection of objects that 'does something' in the game. A door, a train, a planet, a turret or a gun... a ship is a collection of entities and so is a fully loaded player.

Server meshing will drastically reduce the number of entities per server, and Gen12 / Vulkan will allow physics to run on any free thread.

There is a misconception about 'desync' in Star Citizen versus other MMO type games. Star Citizen doesn't work like other games so what appears as 'lag' to the players has a different cause in Star Citizen.

Normally in a multiplayer game the local PC game client directly controls the character and anything the character is using. Once a player initiates an action the effect of that action - the change state - is transmitted up to the server and the change states are then sent down to each player to see in their game client. This means anyone with a slow up/down link shows as game lag to other players, the player sees the result of their actions first and everyone else only sometime afterward.

That is not the way Star Citizen works and once you see why it becomes obvious when you see what happens in the game.

In Star Citizen the player doesn't directly control anything but the player avatar and vehicles. Things like elevators, door controls, loot boxes and even player attachments etc are controlled differently. You give your game client on your PC as command and instead of the client implementing it immediately that command is sent to the server. The server waits a certain period an collects all command requests from connected players within a given time frame (if that time frame is short it 'looks' instant). Once it has waited this set period of time it then sends all those commands out to the connected players so that they see things happen at close to the same time as everyone else. You see the door open on a ship at the same time any other player sees that door open.

Obviously if a server is struggling with a low tick rate the delay between received commands and distributed command results can get longer, and that's why everything seems slow to respond - it can get so slow that players assume it isn't responding at all when if you're prepared to wait, and wait, eventually you might see it respond. Not all player commands require you to input a control, standing in front of an automatic door issues a command request to open that door for you, just as if you had pressed the 'open' command.

There is a time sensitive component, after a long wait the server prioritises other commands - especially if you're trying to give more and more commands in frustration, which is just making it worse - the bottlenecked network becomes even more bottlenecked. Once the server has reached the point it can't respond anymore people start talking about 30K incoming! But there is no 'incoming' for server crash, it either has crashed in which case your game client simply stops implementing commands (from your point of view) until the 'I'm waiting for the server to respond' timer runs out - obviously if the server has actually crashed there won't be any response after the allotted time and so you get the 30K message.

There are times when network commands just don't get through for specific things, like inventory databases and log in hubs, these have nothing to do with the server build, game build or persistence, the line to that database has simply gone down or has become jammed because of the sheer volume of network calls.

There are very good reasons why Star Citizen doesn't work the same way other MMO's do and the system they are building is to take account of what the end goals are, not how the current Alpha plays.

We all made comments about that ISC on server meshing planning and the following day on SC Live Jared admitted that it had not accurately represented the work done, or the current state of play regarding server meshing. As he said.."That was on me".

Work has been done on all the backend systems to support server meshing, and as the page linked above described the actual implementation of meshed DGS's linked to the Replication Layer is not the particularly hard part. Getting all the ducks in a row is the bit that takes the time.

3.18 is the first implementation of the Persistence databases and it has happened, it is working, now it is just a case of bedding it in and dealing with any network issues 'at scale'. Once the build is stabilised they can progress. Log in flow is not about persistence or about meshing servers, the previous implementation of those log in flow's worked but like all new pieces added to a puzzle PES slightly changes the whole picture / balance so that some fine tuning is required. Nothing is 'broken' it just needs a little finessing to get it to work at scale.

You are completely wrong.

A Dedicated Game Server is what the players connect to. There is one Stanton system per server. which takes about 100 players. It has to load all the assets and run all the code for that 100player instance of the game. CIG are now referring to each instance of the game as a shard (from the term used to describe a broken piece of glass) because the plan is to split each instance over several DGS's via server meshing thus reducing the individual DGS workload.

A group of DGS's is called a shard and it is one full instance of the current (now or future) game universe.

Distance is irrelevant. Speed over terrain is an indicator of how well the networking is functioning. Our guy above says that with even minimal assets at walking speed (or even galloping speed) crossing a server boundary there's lag / glitch in the game he mentions.

The number of entities is however very relevant. Each has to cross that boundary in the same state and orientation relative to whatever physics grid it is on / in, with it's attachment point intact. potentially hundreds of thousands of entity values have to cross server boundaries so fast the players don't even notice it has happened. From DGS to persistence database, to replication layer to DGS. All streamed in and out across the network. They even plan to have DGS server recovery happen so fast that if a server crashes that server can be spun up again with everything on it - and take all the entities from another server (remember the data for other planets and moons with all their contents has to be written to the server hard drive first then loaded into memory while the server is running) - and any incoming entities spawning in faster than you can notice that it happened.

Players could be going across a server / server boundary with literally hundreds of entities each, have 10 players with each item port on them filled and an open top box with 50 or so 'essentials' dumped into it on that ship and it could go into thousands of individual entities, multiply that by the number of players taking their own ship full. They plan to physicalise everything. What do you usually have in those other games? 4 or 5 pieces of armour, 3 weapons, a backpack, a shield, maybe a horse and a saddle with 'magic bag of holding' stowed inventory which doesn't need to align to any physics grid.

They plan to have foliage and vegetation affected by wind and engine exhausts. They'll bend in the wind or when an objects collide with them, that state has to be replicated across servers. Water surfaces will also be affected. They've even talked about destructible foliage - and weather, all that is an order of magnitude more difficult than simply static geometry with no collisions at a server /server boundary. Even a dragon you can ride at 5 or 6 m/s with only the things you can carry on you amounts to what? 10 or 12 entities crossing that server boundary? 10 players dragon flying in formation across that boundary all fully loaded is 120 entities... not thousands.

Faced with the possibility of what players may do in a game with no limits the game designers put in limits so that they don't have to do massive investment to overcome those limits. So they have static boundary server meshing, they think - shall we massively overhaul the network code to allow huge amounts of entity transfers per second or shall we put insurmountable hills where the boundaries are with 3 or 4 little single track roads through the boundary hills to control the rate at which players cross those boundaries. We all know what they usually do. That would be the usual solution, it's quicker and cheaper. CIG don't do quick and cheap. Some may find that annoying, why not do it the way other game designers do it and save us the wait? But the freedom it allows players is unprecedented. Comparing a Skyrim type game with static server meshing to what CIG are planning is like comparing a rubber dinghy to an ocean going cruise liner.

Your inventory in another game is stowed in a 'magic bag of holding' - it's player bound, it isn't physicalised, and they don't use Object Container Streaming.

Asking if it's possible or not in a community forum of the game company that says they are going to do it is a bit pointless. Will they do it, everyone has their own opinion but they did get 64Bit precision against the odds, spherical grid planets (there are no spherical grid rotating planets you can fly down to surface in any other game, no matter how much people believe the fakery - and even Starfield won't have that), Object Container Streaming, they did get vehicles inside ships and ships inside ships. They have got tier 1 salvaging and there is a physical interior to ships with cargo boxes visible - all before the workload of servers has been reduced by server meshing.

The point is.. no-one else is even going to try to do those things, let alone at the scale CIG are planning... and that's the point, trying where others fear to tread, it's why it HAD TO BE community funded. No investor in the game industry would attempt it. If the magnitude of what they intend to achieve doesn't absolutely blow your mind then... YOU HAVEN'T UNDERSTOOD IT!

The speed at which the player / game object travels affects at least 3 things:

A. LOD's that have to be loaded (Yes, they are loaded client side for draw calls, but they have to exist on the server too otherwise you get the 'long grass' problem).

B. Object Containers have to be streamed in at a much higher rate and streamed out again the faster you move through an area they have to be matched on each server at server / server boundaries. Persistence has a big effect on this because of how many object containers are put in place by persistence, add that those object containers also have object containers and have to have varying loadouts and even wear and tear - which all comes from networked information from the persistence database. With static geometry and a maximum loadout like in medieval MMO's this workload is both far lower and more predictable. Imagine you flew across a server boundary and your cockpit seat disappears, or your doors, ship components, guns etc because that is what server meshing handles. There's no comparison to a few bit's of armour and a sword being tracked across a server / server boundary.

C. Moving from server to server places load on the network, many objects (far more than a few players / npc's with armour and a sword) crossing server / server boundaries increases that network traffic. If the network isn't fast enough it would be unplayable.

Clouds moving across servers are entities like any other game object, it is not a flat plane with clouds painted onto to it, except there's more... and it adds to that network traffic workload.

The pace of the MMO's mentioned in the same breath as previous versions of Server Meshing are not even close to the volume of tracked objects in Star Citizen, and of course those mentioning other instances are not so hot on the details, like where are those server boundaries? Are they somewhere where there is a lot of activity or far out along some dusty road away from the activity so that the number of entities and activity on those borders is minimal.

No-one is suggesting that Server Meshing hasn't been done before, it's that it hasn't been done on a project this complex - if it had, why are there other similar games with aircraft, vehicles and long range weapons using it now if it's all done and dusted and proven. This is what CIG does, they take known tech and push it far further than most would even try.

SC uses real projectiles (bullets and missiles) that travel in 3D space, they don't use calculated hit points like other games... and the distances those projectiles travel is much further.

Ships that fly at 100m/s over terrain - as opposed to walking, boats or horse riding.
Sniper rifles. tanks and ships that fire a projectile as far as 1km or more - as opposed to spears, bows and arrows or short range magical VFX.
Grids inside of grids inside of grids, all moving independently relative to each other... it's the physics!
Volumetric sky clouds instead of a skybox... imagine clouds crossing server boundaries like the players.

I could go on, but hopefully the point is seen. There's a reason so many open world games are set in the bronze age / iron age magical realms - it's just so much easier.

Have you noticed how these games are always on horseback or on foot? With pointed sticks to fire at each other or balded weapons, ie always with weapons that have no telephoto scope? Draw distances in Star Citizen are much bigger. How many of those games have you flying around in upper atmosphere? Travel over land and air is much faster in SC.

One word.. Dependencies. First Persistence needs to be finalised and then server meshing (the primary reason for server meshing isn't just so that loads of players can all play together, it's to massively decrease the workload required of each DGS, to allow that workload to be spread over many DGS's) as well as Gen12 and Vulkan. The amount of stuff that will come from Squadron 42 to the PU is humongous, there's no way the current server builds and architecture could handle it all without going to 1 tick per second or less. The current server build (one shard per server) is at it's maximum capacity with tick rates going down as low as 4 or 5 per second in some instances, in order to put more in they have to take stuff out. It's still an alpha testing environment, whatever needs testing the most gets priority. Some things don't need a multiplayer environment to test and so it is more efficient to do that in Squadron 42 Episode One.

Some things lend themselves to certain environments, multiplayer inventories, ship selection screens etc are all network dependent so they are best stress tested under heavy player load. Weapon balancing and the flight model don't need heavy player load, that kind of fine tuning can be done by the QA teams with predictable workloads. A PU server load is wholly unpredictable, they can't know in advance what the players are going to bring in, or what they are going to do whereas the Squadron 42 environment is fully predictable, they know exactly what is in each instance and they can repeat those exact same conditions over and over again.

We agree on virtually all except one thing. You ?? seem to think that the running simulation he is showing on the screen is Quantum, but consider this - it isn't Quantum it's his simulation of the planned Live game environment - he's just showing the effects of Quantum on it. Like all CIG dev's Tony doesn't repeat information given previously I was at the 2019 CitizenCon when that simulation was first shown they said it was their live game simulation. There is an important distinction to be made, all the information you have linked to is good, but it all refers to the live service game universe running on shards, but the live service only runs those areas the players are in, there is no subsumption, vNPC's or probability volumes running on the live service in areas where there are no players. The only place where every nook and cranny of the game universe is simulated is in the Quantum simulation.

So yes, the Quanta that is Draygan is spawned into the live simulation as a vNPC interacting with vNPC's in that live game probability volume who can be instantiated as a full NPC in that live environment, and back to a vNPC - as I said. He did say if there was a player in the bar they would see Draygan, no player can enter the Quantum simulation. Also Draygan is removed from the live game once he's done his thing but he is not put into stasis and forgotten, he goes back to the Quantum simulation.

The Quantum simulation runs in parallel with the live game persistent universe, it doesn't control the PU, they both inform each other and other backend services and simulations inform both of them, like Odin and Quasar. If there was no live service running the persistent universe goes on evolving - this is why it is called 'The Living Breathing Universe', it doesn't need players to be there for things to happen, indeed areas where players might not go don't remain static and they don't run on the live service empty of players. If all the NPC's are just one task scripted zombies with no memory they'll still be there even though the place has become a ghost town because all the resources have been mined out in the players absence.

The aim of this project, of both Chris and CIG, is to fake as little as possible, and enable as much player choice as possible... that is what makes it different.

No you may not want to know everything about every person you meet but the one's you choose could be given their attributes 'on the fly' as needed according to what level of interaction you choose with that individual. That is the system they are building, NPC's that evolve as the players interact with them, and as the game universe changes around them. It's why they keep saying ''There are no background characters in Star Citizen''.. it's because they can all evolve to become what the player wants them to become, with full persistence of memory.. not all of them... just the ones the players deem worthy of their attention. Consider the logic - how realistic would it feel to know that every drink can you put down will still be there every time you log in for weeks or months yet every NPC you interact with immediately forgets all about you the minute your back is turned.

No it isn't merely a macro simulation.. if you watched all TZ's presentations - and actually took time to study them - you'd know that.

In his last presentation he gives an example of a Quanta who is a pirate, he attacks virtual NPC's and gains a rep and bounty, he goes drinking in a space station bar where you could physically encounter him and collect that bounty... in the live game, not in a simulation.

Quantum was created to solve a problem of manpower - to create millions of unique NPC's to populate the verse it would take hundreds if not thousands of dev man hours, it is the typical approach CIG takes to problems of scale... Quantum creates those NPC's procedurally according to a formula of personality traits and even backstories and those NPC's are autonomous, they have lives and history logs just like players. They are not scripted scheduled NPC's, the are not virtual NPC's created for missions, they can free roam around the game universe at will.

The tough part people seem not to be able to wrap their heads around is that CIG aren't doing NPC's the standard way (at least not all of them). Usually NPC's are scripted for behaviours by dev's by hand. This is why there is a lot of repetition of AI NPC's in other games. Along with Quantum there are bespoke 'scheduled' NPC's these are the traditional scripted NPC's like bartenders, security staff etc. Then there are virtual NPC's created by mission logic and probability volumes.

Now here's the kicker - all types of AI NPC's have their own lives, character traits, history logs, 24 hour - 7 days a week lives. Tony explained it would be computationally to expensive to have that many NPC's 'live' as entities in the verse so Quantum simulates real individual NPC's in a virtual universe, but if any of those Quanta are in a location to affect either players, scheduled NPC's or even virtual NPC's in the live verse then those individual Quanta are spawned into the game as virtual NPC's or even fully instantiated NPC's - they can transition between each stage as required all while keeping a history of that Quantum character which is stored on a database.

There is a macro economy simulator to run on the backend, I believe it is called Odin, but it is not Quantum that only procedurally generates and runs Quanta individuals either as a simulation (a copy of the live game universe that has no physical entities) or as a resource of a population of formula driven NPC's for the live game (super boosted, on steroids, on demand headless clients). It would be more accurate, and easier to understand, to describe each Quanta as procedurally generated virtual players who when they log out of the live game go back to their virtual life in the simulator, who, just like when real players log out to the real world, are no longer tracked on the live persistence databases until they log back in to the Live game again.

We have a lot of new people in the last 4 or so years, this is a good thing but it has drawbacks. The 'why's' and 'wherefore's' get lost in the mists of time. It is a very complex project to follow so.. a little background for context.

Normally in a game you input a control and the command is executed client-side first then the changed state information is passed up to the servers where everyone else's game client get's updated to show the action the player has just commanded. This causes significant desync. I command door open and it opens, but other players see the door open some time after me. I command 'get out of cockpit seat', but Star Citizen has a fundamental rule, it always assumes someone else should see you get out of your seat at the exact same time you do.

To get around this the engineers fundamentally changed the way Star Citizen executes player commands. Instead of the command being executed immediately by the local game client what happens is the server waits a certain period of time while it gets all player inputs in a locality, then it sends all those player commands down to each connected game client, the theory being all players should see the results of other players inputs at 'closer to' the same time.

Where this appears to fall down from a current players perspective, IE the game appears unresponsive to commands, is when the backend network and servers are struggling. The good news is that we all see the 'stuck-ness' at the same time, the bad news is that due to network foibles and hang-ups everyone thinks the game build is broken because they don't realise Star Citizen doesn't do it the way other games do.. it isn't a game build issue.. the unresponsiveness relates directly to how the backend networking and servers are performing which has a lot to do with how many players are sending inputs in a given time frame.. it's just that players can't fathom why your last command input could take anything up to 1, 3, 5, 10 or even longer minutes to execute. They just think.. it isn't working!

The reason you get stuck in your seat sometimes is the same reason doors won't open and elevators don't come, it's a network snafu. The persistence database isn't just about coffee cups and broken ships littering up the place, the state of the game universe also includes the state of every player and every interactable item. Every time you change anything it gets sent to that database so that it has an exact snapshot of the game world universe at that point in time. This is why random actions seem to work sometimes and not others, it's because the 'change state' information lags behind what those thousands of players are doing. It gets further and further out of sync. According to recent reports, even to the point it becomes unrecoverable.

CIG knows when they have explained something in the past, they don't rehash information for those who aren't up to speed. Their reports of what is happening is comprehensible as long as you have that information, if you don't it just doesn't make sense that the non-working ASOP terminals, the stuck in your seat, the elevators don't come is all directly linked to how the network is passing information to the persistence database and then back down to each connected game client. Players can activate things just by walking up them, like doors, or terminals. It's a command just the same as when you hit a key. They simply say... The state of the game universe is drifting away from what it should be given all those slow or even missed network inputs.

It is the best solution for the unique to Star Citizen issues.. in the long run, once those server overloads and backend services are running optimally.. we just aren't there yet... But it will all come right eventually.

It's all linked to the fundamental way that persistence controls absolutely everything in the game. No-one else has even tried anything like this before. You don't directly control anything in the game, the players make requests and the persistence database makes those requests happen when it 's ready according to the timestamp of the request or the priority of the request (eg. player incapacitated gets priority over player just wants to get up from his seat), for everybody in the game universe, hopefully simultaneously.

People complain, or even scoff at what they are doing but the fact that it works at all, even some of the time, is so 'out there' radically different if it doesn't blow your mind you simply haven't understood it.

One wonders how the project ever got this far based upon that logic. We've had naysayers and detractors predicting doom and gloom since day one... Yet here we are... And here CIG are doing exactly what they said they would.

No-one ever suggested it was going to be easy, if it was easy we'd have the money-suits funding the project waiting for their big pay-off demanding it be dumbed down for an earlier release. It's precisely because none of the industry standard types wanted to know about a project like this that the community stepped up. Blah crowding, blah hard sell tactics.. if the dream wasn't worth a damn good try none of that would have worked.

I agree, they worked through the weekend and even late into the night. We were warned and we knew such a major upgrade / update to the game would probably have a rocky start but they're working through it.

The log in servers are not the AWS regional servers. The account verification phase takes place on CI's own servers. Only once the account is verified are you allowed access the DGS.

You may notice you can only select your region AFTER you have successfully logged in.

Windows update messes with folder permissions if they are within the protected OS environment 'C:/Programs Files' is (it can change them to 'Read only'). It is best to install both the Launcher and the game files into a folder on the root of whatever drive you are using. EG C:/RSI/ or D:/RSI/

In order to do a 'Verify' check (which it does automatically at install) the RSI Launcher has a directory scanner, Windows Defender sees this as suspicious behavior if it is within the protected OS environment, if the directory scan doesn't succeed it will throw an error.

There has been a noticeable 'surge' in RAM use in the latest 2 builds for 3.18. Anyone with less than 8GB VRAM and 32GB RAM is going to struggle, this has unexpected effects since some of the collision physics is done from figures (heightmap values) sent from the GPU to the CPU. My GTX1080Ti is now maxed out (11GB VRAM) while my Ryzen 9 3900X is running at 15%, not really surprised by that, I knew the day would come when the GTX 1080Ti would struggle but hopefully Gen12 optimisations and Vulkan may improve performance. I plan to upgrade to the RX 6800 XT in the near future.

Talking about decoupling Pyro from the need for server meshing there are a couple of possibilities that have been discussed in these forums before, but which were generally not thought to be likely to occur.

1. They allow us to select the star system we want to enter from the main menu - like we select Stanton right now. Obviously this means we can't persist anything except stored ship state and contents into Pyro, no grouping up until you've arrived in system. No continuation of ongoing missions.

2. They make the jump tunnel traversal a loading screen allowing transfer from Stanton DGS's (shards) to Pyro DGS's with the potential for ship state and contents to transfer relying heavily on the enhanced persistence of PES. Some may remember the flight to ground landing zone before pupil to planet was going to be an in-game loading screen, so we know they can do that. This solution does seem to make the comment about the possible issues of persisting missions and player/ship state through the jump gate more understandable or relevant.

The above two possibilities may well suggest to a knowledgeable follower of the project that if this temporary solution was adopted in lieu of server meshing then it suggests they want to give us Pyro and the state of server meshing might delay that entry into Pyro much longer than they were expecting. You don't need a band aid if the gold standard solution is right around the corner.

With server meshing implemented as they have previously described it there wouldn't be any issue of missions persisting through jump gates since you'd remain on the same game universe shard, regeneration does not require body retrieval and yes you might lose your ship and contents if you don't make it through, just like there is no reason not to think missions wouldn't persist after a QT travel between ArcCorp and Hurston. In light of the general discussion - we already know they have added two planetary landing zone teams in the last year and a half. The first 5 years or so were about tool building which takes highly qualified expensive personnel which are not easy to recruit to achieve, once those tools are made however they can recruit newly qualified college kids to make level design using those tools. The guy who make the river tool (which will also be the road and lava tool) is making it so planetary level designers can use it quickly and easily, he doesn't have to make all the rivers, roads and lava flows himself.

Also I can say from experience since I visited F42UK back in 2016, they definitely do not show everything they have worked on the minute it is made. Some of the things we saw no-one else saw them until around 2 years later, yet they make it seem on Youtube like they just made it yesterday. They were very careful to only show us what they wanted us to see, it was obvious from the way they behaved we weren't seeing what they were currently working on. They show one example of something to show that it can be done.

It would be a mistake to assume the only examples of planets, moons, POI's etc they have made are the one's we've seen. The alpha is a testbed and the majority of stuff made - yes that includes 'content' - is for Squadron 42. They have ships and vehicles no-one has seen yet, as well as player tools, weapons, design styles etc. There are not 2 types of cargo crates and 3 types of loot boxes, the one's in the PU are the only one's they have tested. There are more vending machines than the drinks dispenser and Big Benny's. There's a lot more decorative stuff and furniture than we've seen in the PU, there's just no point in dumping it all into the PU because the servers can't handle it all and what's the point in letting people gawp at it for a while then starting asking for more?

Chris Roberts likes to surprise the players, ask any developer if you meet them in person they'll tell you, also game development is a competitive business, if you tell everyone the unique selling points of your game it gives the competition time to make their own versions. There's no need to go shooting themselves in the foot if they don't have to... and they don't have to.

There are good reasons to believe that there are between 10 and 12 star systems in Squadron 42 at least, there are also good reasons to think that the 'building phase' of level design and art asset production for Squadron 42 Episode One has been completed. It is notable that the monthly reports and Roadmap are predominantly about mechanics, not assets. It is also worth pointing out that many assets were made before there even was a Roadmap.

The current PU is a minimal asset test-bed both because of unnecessary 'spoiler' avoidance but also because they have been at the limits of server performance for years and the only thing that is going to drastically change that is server meshing. No matter what the constantly negative naysayers tell you, they haven't sat on their butts twiddling their thumbs waiting for server meshing. The fact that they have been heavily recruiting artists and level designers in the last 2 years should tell you something.
